Key Components of Hume's Associationist Theory
Author presents Hume's theory and its epistemological categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four key components of Hume's theory: impressions, ideas, facts, and additions.

Core Idea
Hume reduces all knowledge to sensory impressions; ideas are mere copies.

Hume's theory is a radical form of empiricism. To critique it, you must first identify its building blocks: impressions, ideas, facts, and additions. This lesson walks you through locating these terms in the passage and understanding their relationships.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of impressions
Find where the passage defines 'impressions' (al-inṭibāʿāt). It says the mind is an instrument for receiving impressions from the senses. Note that impressions are vivid and primary.
2
Find the definition of ideas
Look for the description of 'ideas' (al-afkār). The passage calls them weak, faint images. Ideas are copies of impressions, not original.
3
Identify the distinction between facts and additions
The passage divides human knowledge into 'facts' (al-waqāʾiʿ) from the external world and 'additions' (al-iḍāfāt) which are negations of facts. This is Hume's classification.
Additions may include logical relations or fictions
4
Trace the implication for abstract ideas
The passage states that any abstract idea not supported by a sense should be doubted. This is a key consequence: Hume rejects non-sensory knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦ - نظرية تداعي المعاني رائدها فيلسوف اسكتلندة الاكبر دافيد هيوم (١٧۱۱-١٧٧٦م) وتقول (ان الذهن هو مجرد اداة استقبال الانطباعات من الحواس . والافكار في الذهن هي صور ضعيفة خافتة وعندما تأتي انطباعات جديدة شديدة فانها تقوم بزيادة أو انقاص الصور الضعيفة. نحن نفكر على اساس البيانات التي تقدمها لنا الحواس واي نقص في احد اعضاء الحس يوازيه نقص في عالم الافكار. واي افكار مجردة لم يكن لها حس يدعمها علينا الشك في قيمتها . ان الانطباعات ترتبط فيما بينها بارتباط منظم وهذا الارتباط نلاحظه بوضوح في حالات الانتباه الشديدة وفي الاحلام والخيال . ان المعرفة البشرية هي وقائع واضافات . الوقائع تاتي من العالم الخارجي والاضافات هي نقائض الوقائع ١ فيلسوف ومؤرخ انجليزي لديه افكار خاصة يقول فيها ان العناية الالهية وخلود النفس وسائر صفات الله وكل قصة الخلق التي تؤمن بها الديانات هي مجرد خرافة . انظر الموسوعة الفلسفية، عبدالرحمن بدوي، مصدر سابق ج ٢ ص ٦١٢
16. The Theory of Association of Ideas. Its pioneer is the greatest Scottish philosopher David Hume (1711-1776 CE). It says: the mind is merely an instrument for receiving impressions from the senses. Ideas in the mind are weak, faint images. When new, strong impressions come, they increase or decrease the weak images. We think on the basis of data that the senses provide us. Any deficiency in one of the sense organs is paralleled by a deficiency in the world of ideas. Any abstract ideas not supported by a sense – we should doubt their value. Impressions are connected among themselves by an organized connection, and this connection we clearly observe in states of intense attention, in dreams and imagination. Human knowledge consists of facts and additions. Facts come from the external world; additions are negations of facts. 1. An English philosopher and historian with particular ideas in which he says that divine providence, the immortality of the soul, all of God's attributes, and every story of creation that religions believe in are mere superstition. See: Philosophical Encyclopedia, 'Abd al-Rahman Badawi, previous source, vol. 2, p. 612
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage introduces David Hume's associationist theory of knowledge. According to Hume, the mind is passive: it receives impressions from the senses, and ideas are faded copies of these impressions. Thinking relies entirely on sensory data; a missing sense leads to missing ideas. Abstract ideas without sensory grounding are to be doubted. Impressions are linked by association, observable in concentration, dreams, and imagination. Human knowledge is divided into 'facts' (from the external world) and 'additions' (negations of facts). The footnote reveals Hume's materialistic and atheistic views, dismissing divine providence, immortality, and creation stories as superstition. The passage presents the theory without immediate critique, but the tone suggests its inadequacy, as the earlier lesson already critiqued empiricism.

Key Terms
الانطباعات
الافكار
الوقائع
الاضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should we do with abstract ideas that have no sensory support?
A) Accept them as a priori truths.
B) Doubt their value.
C) Seek rational proof for them.
D) Consider them as additions to facts.

Key Takeaway
Hume's epistemology reduces all knowledge to sensory impressions, rejecting any knowledge not grounded in sense experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which component of Hume's theory (impressions, ideas, facts, or additions) would correspond to the Islamic belief in the angels, and explain why Hume's system would reject it as invalid knowledge.
?
Compare Hume's division of knowledge into facts and additions with the Islamic classification of knowledge into 'ilm al-yaqīn (certain knowledge) and ẓann (conjecture). Where would Hume's 'additions' fall?

Critiquing Hume's Dismissal of Religious Truths
Author exposes Hume's atheistic conclusions derived from his epistemology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Hume's argument against religious truths and evaluate it using Islamic epistemological principles.

Core Idea
Hume's argument fails because Islamic knowledge includes revelation and necessary rational truths.

The footnote in the passage reveals the ultimate consequence of Hume's theory: he dismisses divine providence, attributes, and creation as superstition. This lesson guides you in extracting his implied argument and refuting it from within an Islamic epistemological framework.

Guided Path
1
Extract Hume's conclusion from the footnote
The footnote states that divine providence, immortality, attributes, and creation stories are 'mere superstition'. This is his conclusion about religious truths.
2
Identify the unstated premise
Hume's conclusion rests on his theory: only sensory impressions produce valid knowledge. Any claim not traceable to impressions is valueless. This is the hidden major premise.
3
Contrast with Islamic sources of knowledge
In Islam, knowledge comes from senses, reason ('aql), and revelation (waḥy). Revelation provides certain truth about the unseen (ghayb) that the senses cannot reach.
For example, knowledge of Allah's attributes is from revelation and rational necessity.
4
Evaluate the inner coherence of Hume's critique
Hume's own theory is not derived from sense impressions (it is an abstract idea about knowledge). By his own standard, it should be doubted. This self-refutation reveals the flaw.

Source Text

16. The Theory of Association of Ideas. Its pioneer is the greatest Scottish philosopher David Hume (1711-1776 CE). It says: the mind is merely an instrument for receiving impressions from the senses. Ideas in the mind are weak, faint images. When new, strong impressions come, they increase or decrease the weak images. We think on the basis of data that the senses provide us. Any deficiency in one of the sense organs is paralleled by a deficiency in the world of ideas. Any abstract ideas not supported by a sense – we should doubt their value. Impressions are connected among themselves by an organized connection, and this connection we clearly observe in states of intense attention, in dreams and imagination. Human knowledge consists of facts and additions. Facts come from the external world; additions are negations of facts. 1. An English philosopher and historian with particular ideas in which he says that divine providence, the immortality of the soul, all of God's attributes, and every story of creation that religions believe in are mere superstition. See: Philosophical Encyclopedia, 'Abd al-Rahman Badawi, previous source, vol. 2, p. 612

Commentary
This passage introduces David Hume's associationist theory of knowledge. According to Hume, the mind is passive: it receives impressions from the senses, and ideas are faded copies of these impressions. Thinking relies entirely on sensory data; a missing sense leads to missing ideas. Abstract ideas without sensory grounding are to be doubted. Impressions are linked by association, observable in concentration, dreams, and imagination. Human knowledge is divided into 'facts' (from the external world) and 'additions' (negations of facts). The footnote reveals Hume's materialistic and atheistic views, dismissing divine providence, immortality, and creation stories as superstition. The passage presents the theory without immediate critique, but the tone suggests its inadequacy, as the earlier lesson already critiqued empiricism.

Checkpoint
What does Hume's footnote claim about divine attributes?
A) They are rationally provable.
B) They are mere superstition.
C) They are facts from the external world.
D) They are additions to facts.

Key Takeaway
Hume's epistemology leads to rejection of divine truths, but his own theory is self-refuting and cannot account for knowledge from reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the Islamic classification of knowledge (sensed, rational, revealed), construct a refutation of Hume's claim that belief in God's attributes is superstition. Cite one rational proof from Kalam.
?
Identify the self-refuting nature of Hume's empiricism: his own statement 'only sense impressions give knowledge' is not a sense impression. Explain why this invalidates his entire theory.

Counterexamples to the Three-Stage Theory of Knowledge
Author refutes the universal application of the three-stage theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify counterexamples to the three-stage theory of knowledge and evaluate the strength of the critique.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge comes through sensory stages—mathematical truths and ethical intuitions are innate.

The author challenges the claim that all knowledge must pass through sensory, scientific, and philosophical stages. By examining mathematics and intuitive knowledge, you will see why this theory fails. This lesson equips you to recognise such counterexamples and defend innate knowledge in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Find in the Arabic the assertion that all knowledge passes through three stages: sensory, scientific, philosophical. Note that the author rejects this universality.
The target is the claim that these stages are necessary for all knowledge.
2
Extract the first counterexample
The author cites principles of mathematics (e.g., 1+1=2). These are timeless truths, known since the dawn of thought, and cannot be assigned to any particular stage. Why does this refute the theory?
Mathematics is a priori, not derived from sensory experience.
3
Extract the second counterexample
The author points to intuitive knowledge (ma'arif wijdaniyya), such as love of good and hatred of evil. These are innate and do not pass through empirical stages.
These are ethical intuitions, often linked to fitra in Islamic thought.
4
Assess the author's conclusion
The author concludes that the scientific theory of knowledge only describes the external form of some knowledge, failing to reach the roots of thought or the fountain of perception. Does this conclusion follow from the counterexamples?
The argument is: a universal claim fails if any counterexample exists.
5
Evaluate the critique's validity
Consider whether the critique itself is logically sound. Are there any hidden assumptions? How might a defender of the three-stage theory respond?
The critique relies on the accepted nature of mathematical and ethical truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ (لا يمكن القطع بان جميع المعارف تمر بالمراحل الثلاث المذكورة آنفا) ان مباديء الرياضيات لا يمكن ان تمر بالمراحل الحسية والعلمية والفلسفية ذلك ان ۱ ۱ - ۲ سواءا كنت عالما أم جاهلا أم شاعرا ام كاتباء ان هذه الحقيقة مثلا راسخة منذ فجر التفكير البشري ولا يمكن تصنيفها الى أي مرحلة. وكذلك المعارف الوجدانية كحب الخير وبغض الشر وحب الجمال وبغض القبح الخ والخلاصة : ان نظرية المعرفة العلمية تصف الشكل الخارجي لبعض حالات المعرفة من دون ان تصل الى الخيوط الاولية للتفكير أو الينبوع العام للادراك ان هذه النظرية السطحية لاتعطي تفسيرا لسؤال هذه الدراسة وهو كيف تدرك النفس حقائق الاشياء ؟
4. It is not possible to assert that all knowledge passes through the aforementioned three stages. The principles of mathematics cannot pass through the sensory, scientific, and philosophical stages, because this truth, for example, is firmly established since the dawn of human thought and cannot be classified into any stage. Likewise, intuitive knowledge, such as love of good and hatred of evil, love of beauty and hatred of ugliness, etc. In summary: the scientific theory of knowledge describes the external form of some cases of knowledge without reaching the primary threads of thought or the general fountain of perception. This superficial theory does not provide an answer to the question of this study: How does the soul perceive the realities of things?
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the claim that all knowledge necessarily passes through three sequential stages: sensory, scientific, and philosophical. The author argues that this theory is not universally applicable. First, the principles of mathematics are presented as a counterexample: they are timeless truths that have been known since the dawn of human thought and cannot be assigned to any particular stage. Second, intuitive knowledge (ma'arif wijdaniyya), such as the love of good and hatred of evil, are also not subject to such classification; they are innate and independent of empirical stages. The author concludes that the scientific theory of knowledge only describes the external form of some knowledge cases but fails to reach the fundamental roots of thought or the general source of perception. Thus, it cannot answer the central question of how the soul perceives the realities of things. The critique uses examples from ethics (love of good, hatred of evil) which align with Islamic ethical intuitions.

Key Terms
المعارف الوجدانية
المراحل الثلاث
نظرية المعرفة العلمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of knowledge cannot be classified into the three stages?
A) Scientific facts discovered through experimentation.
B) Principles of mathematics and intuitive knowledge like love of good.
C) Historical narratives passed down through generations.
D) Philosophical arguments about metaphysics.

Key Takeaway
The three-stage theory of knowledge fails because it cannot account for innate mathematical and ethical truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify another example from Islamic thought of knowledge that does not pass through the three stages (e.g., the innate knowledge of Tawhid according to the fitra). Write a short paragraph using the author's reasoning to argue why the three-stage theory cannot account for this knowledge.
?
In a debate with someone who claims all knowledge comes from the senses, construct a counterargument using the author's examples from mathematics and intuitive ethics. Use only Islamic examples.

Hume's Empiricism: A Contrast to Islamic Epistemology
Author presents Hume's empiricist epistemology as a foil to Islamic thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key tenets of Hume's empiricist theory and contrast them with the Islamic position on the sources of knowledge.

Core Idea
Hume reduces all knowledge to sensation; Islam affirms revealed and rational knowledge beyond senses.

In this lesson, we examine David Hume's theory of association of ideas, which reduces all human knowledge to sensory impressions and their associations. This theory stands in sharp contrast to Islamic epistemology, which recognizes multiple sources of knowledge including revelation (waḥy), innate disposition (fiṭra), and sound reason ('aql). By understanding Hume's claims, you will better appreciate the distinctive features of the Islamic epistemological framework.

Guided Path
1
Identify Hume's claim about the mind and ideas
Find where the passage says the mind is a receiver of impressions and ideas are faint copies. This establishes his empiricist foundation.
The passage states: 'the mind is merely a tool for receiving impressions from the senses. Ideas in the mind are weak, faint images.'
2
Extract Hume's division of knowledge
Locate the distinction between 'matters of fact' and 'relations of ideas'. Note that matters of fact come from the external world, while relations are negations of facts.
This division is central to Hume's epistemology; it limits a priori knowledge to relations of ideas.
3
Analyze Hume's view on causation
Look for the billiard ball example and the claim that cause and effect are not necessary but habitual. Recognize that he denies rational necessity in causal relations.
The passage says 'nothing shows us that steam results from applying heat to liquid except the relationship between causes and effects that become clear to us through habit, repetition, and experience.'
4
Contrast with Islamic epistemology
Reflect on how Islamic thought differs: knowledge includes necessary truths (e.g., law of contradiction) and revealed truths. Causation is not mere habit but depends on Allah's will as argued by Ash'ari theologians.
In Islamic kalam, causation is contingent on Allah's creation of habits (ʿāda), not on empirical association alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٦ - نظرية تداعي المعاني رائدها فيلسوف اسكتلندة الاكبر دافيد هيوم (١٧۱۱-١٧٧٦م) وتقول (ان الذهن هو مجرد اداة استقبال الانطباعات من الحواس . والافكار في الذهن هي صور ضعيفة خافتة وعندما تأتي انطباعات جديدة شديدة فانها تقوم بزيادة أو انقاص الصور الضعيفة. نحن نفكر على اساس البيانات التي تقدمها لنا الحواس واي نقص في احد اعضاء الحس يوازيه نقص في عالم الافكار. واي افكار مجردة لم يكن لها حس يدعمها علينا الشك في قيمتها . ان الانطباعات ترتبط فيما بينها بارتباط منظم وهذا الارتباط نلاحظه بوضوح في حالات الانتباه الشديدة وفي الاحلام والخيال . ان المعرفة البشرية هي وقائع واضافات . الوقائع تاتي من العالم الخارجي والاضافات هي نقائض الوقائع. فالقوانين تكتشف بالتجربة ولا يمكن اكتشافها بالتأمل النظري وحتى العلة والمعلول هي امور تقررها وقائع الطبيعة ذاتها فلا شيء يبين لنا ان البخار ينتج من توجيه الحرارة الى السائل الا العلاقة بين الاسباب والآثار التي تتوضح لنا من نتاج العادة والتكرار والتجربة . فالطفل الذي تحترق يده حين يقربها من النار نراه يحجم ثانية عن ان يفعل الفعل نفسه والعادة اكبر منار في حياة الانسان اما الاعتقاد فهو نتاج تجارب سابقة ولاحقة ذكريات واحساسات جديدة اضعف شدة واقوى شدة أما معيار التمييز بين الحقائق والاوهام فهو ان الاوهام لاتلاقي الطبيعة ولاتنسجم معها في حين ان الحقائق تلاقي الطبيعة وتنسجم معها وتلائمها . ان جوهر التفكير هو الحس ومباديء التداعي بين الافكار (انك) ترى كرة البلياردو تتحرك فتصادف كرة اخرى فتتحرك هذه ، وليس في حركة الاولى ما يدل على ضرورة تحرك الثانية. فاذا قامت عملية عقلية فانها تستدعي دائما عملية اخرى تتبعها من دون تخلف فينمو بين العمليتين بمضي الزمان رابطة قوية هي رابطة تداعي المعاني وهكذا هي حركة الافكار ) ( والدين في حقيقته ضرب من الفلسفة فالاساس هو الحس ومباديء التداعي بين الافكار اما حياة ما بعد الموت فهي فكرة خيالية لا يوجد احساس سابق يؤيد وجودها اما الاخلاق فهي ناتجة عن -١ -التعاطف بين الناس ٢- عادات تيسر للانسان العيش - تعبيرات عن الغرائز المشتركة بين الناس جميعا ويلاحظ على هذه النظرية :
16. The theory of association of ideas, pioneered by the greatest Scottish philosopher David Hume (1711-1776), says: The mind is merely a tool for receiving impressions from the senses. Ideas in the mind are weak, faint images. When new strong impressions come, they increase or decrease the weak images. We think based on the data provided by the senses, and any deficiency in a sense organ is paralleled by a deficiency in the world of ideas. Any abstract ideas that lack sensory support must be doubted. Impressions are connected among themselves by an organized connection, and this connection is clearly observed in states of intense attention, dreams, and imagination. Human knowledge consists of matters of fact and relations of ideas. Matters of fact come from the external world, and relations of ideas are negations of matters of fact. Laws are discovered by experience and cannot be discovered by theoretical reflection. Even cause and effect are matters determined by the facts of nature themselves; nothing shows us that steam results from applying heat to liquid except the relationship between causes and effects that become clear to us through habit, repetition, and experience. The child whose hand burns when he approaches fire is seen to refrain from doing the same act again. Habit is the greatest beacon in human life. Belief is the product of previous and subsequent experiences, memories and new sensations, weaker and stronger in intensity. The criterion for distinguishing truth from illusion is that illusions do not meet nature and are not consistent with it, while truths meet nature, are consistent with it, and harmonize with it. The essence of thinking is sense and the principles of association of ideas. (You see a billiard ball move and hit another ball, then it moves; in the movement of the first there is no indication of the necessity of the movement of the second. If a mental process occurs, it always calls another process that follows it without delay, and over time a strong bond grows between the two processes, which is the bond of association of ideas. Thus is the movement of ideas.) Religion in its essence is a kind of philosophy; the basis is sense and the principles of association of ideas. Life after death is an imaginary idea for which no prior sensation supports its existence. Morality results from 1) sympathy among people, 2) customs that facilitate human life, 3) expressions of instincts common to all people. The following is observed about this theory:
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage presents the theory of association of ideas (tadā'ī al-ma'ānī) as articulated by David Hume, the Scottish empiricist. The author first identifies Hume as the pioneer. The theory holds that the mind is merely a receiver of sensory impressions; ideas are faint copies of these impressions. Thinking is entirely dependent on sensory data, and any idea without sensory support is suspect. Impressions are interconnected by a regular association, observable in attention, dreams, and imagination. Knowledge is divided into matters of fact (from the world) and relations of ideas (negations of facts). Laws of nature are discovered through experience, not theoretical reflection. Cause and effect are not necessary connections but products of habit and repetition. The example of the child and fire illustrates how habit governs behavior. Belief is built from accumulated experiences. The criterion for truth is consistency with nature. The essence of thought is sense and association. The billiard ball example illustrates that no necessary connection is perceived between cause and effect; association creates the expectation. The theory is then applied to religion (as a kind of philosophy based on sense) and morality (rooted in sympathy, custom, and instinct). The passage ends with an indication of an observation (the author's critique) but is cut off. The text is a straightforward exposition of Hume's empiricist epistemology, which stands in contrast to Islamic views on innate knowledge and the role of reason.

Key Terms
تداعي المعاني
الانطباعات
الوقائع والاضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Hume identify as the criterion for distinguishing truth from illusion?
A) Consistency with sense experience and nature.
B) Agreement with divine revelation.
C) Clarity and distinctness of the idea.
D) The strength of the associated impression.

Key Takeaway
Hume's empiricism limits knowledge to sensory impressions and habitual associations, contrasting sharply with the Islamic affirmation of revealed and rationally necessary knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare Hume's claim that 'belief is the product of previous experiences' with the Islamic position that belief (īmān) is based on both rational conviction and divine guidance. Write a short paragraph highlighting the key difference.
?
Identify a statement from the passage that Hume would consider a 'relation of ideas' (e.g., a logical truth) and one that is a 'matter of fact'. Then, for each, give an equivalent example from Islamic theology (e.g., the law of non-contradiction as a relation, and the existence of prophets as a matter of fact).

Hume's Empiricist Epistemology: Causality as Habit
Author presents Hume's empirical theory reducing knowledge to sense and habitual association.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key epistemological claims of Humean empiricism and contrast them with the Islamic view of causality and knowledge.

Core Idea
Hume reduces causality to habit; Islamic logic affirms necessary connection grounded in divine causation.

David Hume's empiricist epistemology challenges the foundational assumptions of Islamic philosophy, particularly regarding causality and the source of knowledge. In this passage, the author summarizes Hume's reduction of all knowledge to sense experience and the habitual association of ideas. Understanding this contrast is crucial for engaging with modern critiques of Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
Find the statement that says all laws are discovered by experience, not theoretical reflection.
2
Analyze the causality example
Notice the child and fire example: how does Hume explain the child's behavior? What role does habit play?
3
Examine the truth criterion
What is the criterion for distinguishing truth from illusion according to the passage?
4
Apply to religion and ethics
How does Hume extend his empiricism to religion and ethics? Identify the claims about life after death and morality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقوانين تكتشف بالتجربة ولا يمكن اكتشافها بالتأمل النظري وحتى العلة والمعلول هي امور تقررها وقائع الطبيعة ذاتها فلا شيء يبين لنا ان البخار ينتج من توجيه الحرارة الى السائل الا العلاقة بين الاسباب والآثار التي تتوضح لنا من نتاج العادة والتكرار والتجربة . فالطفل الذي تحترق يده حين يقربها من النار نراه يحجم ثانية عن ان يفعل الفعل نفسه والعادة اكبر منار في حياة الانسان اما الاعتقاد فهو نتاج تجارب سابقة ولاحقة ذكريات واحساسات جديدة اضعف شدة واقوى شدة أما معيار التمييز بين الحقائق والاوهام فهو ان الاوهام لاتلاقي الطبيعة ولاتنسجم معها في حين ان الحقائق تلاقي الطبيعة وتنسجم معها وتلائمها . ان جوهر التفكير هو الحس ومباديء التداعي بين الافكار ' (انك) ترى كرة البلياردو تتحرك فتصادف كرة اخرى فتتحرك هذه ، وليس في حركة الاولى ما يدل على ضرورة تحرك الثانية. فاذا قامت عملية عقلية فانها تستدعي دائما عملية اخرى تتبعها من دون تخلف فينمو بين العمليتين بمضي الزمان رابطة قوية هي رابطة تداعي المعاني وهكذا هي حركة الافكار ) ( والدين في حقيقته ضرب من الفلسفة فالاساس هو الحس ومباديء التداعي بين الافكار اما حياة ما بعد الموت فهي فكرة خيالية لا يوجد احساس سابق يؤيد وجودها اما الاخلاق فهي ناتجة عن -١ -التعاطف بين الناس ٢- عادات تيسر للانسان العيش - تعبيرات عن الغرائز المشتركة بين الناس جميعا ويلاحظ على هذه النظرية :
Laws are discovered by experience and cannot be discovered by theoretical reflection. Even cause and effect are matters determined by the facts of nature themselves; nothing shows us that steam is produced by applying heat to liquid except the relationship between causes and effects that becomes clear to us from the product of habit, repetition, and experience. The child whose hand burns when he brings it near fire, we see him refrain a second time from doing the same act. Habit is the greatest beacon in human life. As for belief, it is the product of previous and subsequent experiences, memories and new sensations, weaker or stronger in intensity. As for the criterion for distinguishing truth from illusion: illusions do not conform to nature and are not consistent with it, while truths conform to nature, are consistent with it, and are compatible with it. The essence of thought is sense and the principles of association of ideas. 'You see a billiard ball move, then strike another ball, then this one moves, and there is nothing in the motion of the first that indicates the necessity of the second's motion. If a mental process occurs, it always calls for another process that follows it without fail; over time a strong bond grows between the two processes—the bond of association of meanings—and such is the motion of ideas.' Religion in its essence is a kind of philosophy; the foundation is sense and the principles of association of ideas. As for life after death, it is an imaginary idea for which no previous sensation supports its existence. As for ethics, they result from: 1) sympathy among people, 2) habits that facilitate human life—expressions of instincts common to all people. Observations on this theory:
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents a summary of David Hume's empiricist epistemology as understood by the author. It begins by stating that all laws are discovered through experience, not theoretical reflection, and even the relation of cause and effect is determined by natural facts. The example of steam from heat illustrates that only habitual observation shows the connection. The child's withdrawal from fire demonstrates the role of habit. Belief is assembled from previous and later experiences and sensations. The criterion for truth is conformity with nature. The essence of thought is sense and association of ideas, illustrated by the billiard ball example: seeing one ball move and hit another does not prove necessary connection; the mind expects the second to move only due to habitual association. The passage extends this to religion, considering it a kind of philosophy based on sense and association, and rejects life after death as lacking sensory basis. Ethics are derived from sympathy and habits. This sets up Hume's views before critiquing them.

Key Terms
التجربة
العلة والمعلول
التداعي بين الافكار
العادة

Key Takeaway
Hume reduces causality to habitual association, denying necessary connection, which fundamentally challenges Islamic philosophical theology.

The Disjunctive Critique of Hume's Law of Association
Author critiques Hume's law of association via a disjunctive dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive argument against Hume's law of association and evaluate its force.

Core Idea
Hume's own principle is put on the horns of a dilemma: a priori or unstable.

In this lesson, we examine the first critique of Hume's empiricism. The author uses a disjunctive argument to challenge the foundational status of the law of association. By analyzing this argument, you will see how a simple logical form can expose a deep inconsistency in an entire epistemological system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
What are the two alternatives presented? The law is either a primary fixed mental truth or it is mutable.
2
Trace the consequences of each horn
If it is a fixed a priori law, then primary laws exist. If it is mutable, then it is not a stable foundation.
3
Assess the dilemma's force
Does this disjunction genuinely exhaust the possibilities? What would Hume say?
The author assumes the disjunction is exhaustive; a possible middle ground is that the law is empirically derived yet stable.
4
Connect to Islamic epistemology
How does this critique align with the Islamic doctrine of a priori principles (awwaliyat)?
In Islamic thought, primary truths like 'the whole is greater than the part' are foundational; Hume's denial is thus problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۱ ) (ان) قانون تداعي المعاني نفسه هل هو حقيقة اولية عقلية ثابتة او قابلة للذوبان والتداعي في معان جديدة فاذا كان قانونا عقليا اوليا ثابتا فقد ثبت وجود القوانين الاولية واذا كان لم يتكامل حتى اليوم شكل القانون الاولي فهو ليس جديرا بالدراسة لانه لم يكتسب صفة ما (١)
1) The law of association of ideas itself: is it a primary, fixed, mental truth, or is it subject to dissolution and association into new meanings? If it is a primary, fixed mental law, then the existence of primary laws is established. But if it has not yet become complete as a primary law, then it is not worthy of study because it has not acquired any quality.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the first critique of Hume's empiricism using a disjunctive argument. The author questions the status of the law of association itself: either it is a primary, fixed mental principle (a priori), or it is mutable and can dissolve into new associations. If it is a fixed a priori principle, then that proves the existence of such primary laws—contradicting Hume's claim that all knowledge comes from experience. If it is not yet complete as a primary law, then it lacks stability and is not a reliable foundation for knowledge. Thus, Hume's own principle undermines itself. The argument forces a dilemma: either accept a priori principles or reject the law of association as a valid basis for epistemology.

Key Terms
قانون تداعي المعاني
حقيقة اولية عقلية ثابتة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows if the law of association is a primary fixed mental law?
A) It is mutable
B) The existence of primary laws is established
C) It is not worthy of study
D) It cannot be known

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive argument can test the self-consistency of a foundational principle by showing that each alternative undermines the theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the premise: 'Either the Quran is created or uncreated.' Using the disjunctive form from this lesson, show how a Mutakallim (theologian) would argue that both horns lead to a conclusion that supports the orthodox position (that the Quran is uncreated).
?
Construct a disjunctive critique of an empirical claim, e.g., 'Either human reason is sufficient for ethics or revelation is needed.' Model your argument on the structure in this passage.

Critique of Hume's Associationism via Stability
Author argues the law of association would destroy stability of principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the critique of Hume's associationism by testing its logical consequence against observed stability.

Core Idea
Hume's associationism would make all knowledge unstable; but it isn't, so it's false.

In this critique, the author identifies a devastating implication of David Hume's law of association: if all thought arises from mere association of ideas, then no principle — scientific, logical, ethical, or mathematical — could be stable. But we observe that such principles have remained unchanged for centuries. The argument is a powerful reductio ad absurdum that challenges the very foundation of Hume's epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Clearly state Hume's law of association as the claim that all thought originates from the continuous association of meanings.
2
State the logical consequence
If this law were true, then all scientific, logical, ethical, and mathematical principles would be unstable and change constantly.
3
Observe the real-world evidence
Notice that concepts like good/evil, truth/falsehood, beauty/ugliness, and mathematical axioms have remained stable for centuries.
4
Apply modus tollens
If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P is the law of association, Q is instability. Since Q is false, P must be false.
5
Conclude the critique
Thus the law of association cannot be the basis of human thought, as claimed by Hume.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲) ان هذا القانون يعني ان القوانين العلمية والمنطقية والاخلاقية والرياضية تتهشم كل حين لانها لاتمتلك صفة الثبات بسبب هذا التداعي المستمر للمعاني . وهذا غير حاصل لان مفاهيم مثل الخير والشر والخطأ والصواب والجمال والقبح او المباديء الرياضية لم يشملها قانون التداعي مئات خلت من السنين حتى يومنا هذا . مما يثبت ان التداعي في المعاني ليس اساس ولادة الفكر البشري
2) This law means that scientific, logical, ethical, and mathematical laws shatter every moment because they do not possess the quality of stability due to this continuous association of meanings. But this does not happen, because concepts such as good and evil, false and correct, beauty and ugliness, or mathematical principles have not been touched by the law of association for hundreds of years up to this day. This proves that the association of meanings is not the basis for the birth of human thought.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the second critique: if the law of association were the sole mechanism of thought, then all principles would be in constant flux, as associations shift. Yet in reality, fundamental ethical and mathematical concepts (good/evil, truth/falsehood, beauty/ugliness, mathematical axioms) have remained stable for centuries. This stability contradicts Hume's claim that thought is entirely generated by association. Therefore, association cannot be the origin of human thought. The argument uses a reductio ad absurdum: the consequence of Hume's view is instability, but stability is observed, so the premise is false.

Key Terms
القوانين العلمية والمنطقية والاخلاقية والرياضية
التداعي المستمر للمعاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is used as evidence against Hume's law of association in the passage?
A) The instability of scientific laws
B) The stability of ethical and mathematical concepts over centuries
C) The empirical origin of all ideas
D) The necessity of causal connections

Key Takeaway
The stability of fundamental principles over time proves that the law of association cannot be the origin of human thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reconstruct the author's argument as a conditional syllogism (modus tollens). Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the exception.
?
Apply the same logical critique to the claim: 'If all knowledge comes only from sense experience, then mathematical truths would be uncertain.' Use the stability of mathematical truths to refute that claim.

Hume's Implicit Admission of Necessary Connection
Author argues Hume implicitly admits non-empirical necessity and that causality is a concept not derived from sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr uses the concept of necessity to critique Hume's empiricism and recognize the non-empirical origin of causality.

Core Idea
Hume's critique of necessity self-destructs: he admits a non-empirical relation.

This passage delivers the third critique of Hume's philosophy of causality. Al-Sadr points out an internal contradiction: Hume implicitly admits a necessary relation that cannot be derived from sensory experience. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows how the concept of causality is a rational, a priori notion.

Guided Path
1
Locate Hume's implicit admission
Find where the passage states that Hume concedes a non-empirical relation (الضرورة) between ideas. This is the key contradiction.
The phrase 'اعترف ضمنا' marks the admission.
2
Identify the logical problem
Hume's admission of necessity undermines his own empiricism because necessity cannot be sensed. If causality were only succession, his project fails.
The author says 'لم يتحقق لهيوم ما اراد'.
3
Extract the author's alternative account
Al-Sadr asserts that causality is a 'third idea' not from senses; the mind (الذهن) extracts it. Sensations provide no data for this extraction.
Contrast: Hume says causality comes from habit; al-Sadr says from mind.
4
Evaluate the critique's force
Consider whether al-Sadr's argument successfully shows Hume's position is self-defeating. The passage claims Hume cannot escape a priori necessity.
Think about the role of 'ضرورة' in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۳ ) (ان هيوم اعترف ضمنا بوجود علاقة غير تجريبية بين فكرتين وهي علاقة الضرورة. فان الضرورة سواء أكانت بين فكرتين أم بين واقعين موضوعيين لا يمكن اثباتها بالتجربة الحسية واذا كانت العلاقة مجرد مقارنة فلم يتحقق لهيوم ما اراد من تفسير عنصر الضرورة في قانون العلة والمعلول والحقيقة التي لا مجال لانكارها هي اننا نتصور مفهوم العلية سواء أصدقنا به ام لا وليس تصور العلية مركبا من تصور لشيئين متعاقبين بل هي فكرة ثالثة لم تدرك بالحس ولولا وجود العلية فمن اين ادرك هيوم هذه العلاقة الموضوعية بين الاشياء ؟ كيف تصور هيوم هذا القانون ؟ ان الذهن هو الذي انتزع العلاقة ولم تقدم الاحساسات أي معطيات في هذا الانتزاع ومجمل القول ١ فلسفتنا محمد باقر الصدر، مصدر سابق ص ٦٧ ۲ فلسفتنا محمد باقر الصدر، مصدر سابق ص ۷۸ المصدر نفسه ص ٦٦
3) Hume implicitly admitted the existence of a non-empirical relation between two ideas, namely the relation of necessity. For necessity, whether between two ideas or between two objective realities, cannot be proven by sensory experience. And if the relation were merely comparison, then Hume did not achieve what he wanted in explaining the element of necessity in the law of cause and effect. The undeniable truth is that we conceive of the concept of causality whether we believe in it or not. The conception of causality is not composed of the conception of two successive things; rather it is a third idea not perceived by the senses. And were it not for causality, from where did Hume perceive this objective relation between things? How did Hume conceive this law? The mind extracted the relation; sensations provided no data in this extraction. In summary: (footnotes to Muhammad Baqir al-Sadr's works).
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage delivers the third critique by pointing out an internal inconsistency in Hume's position. Hume supposedly admitted implicitly that there is a non-empirical relation between two ideas—the relation of necessity. But if necessity cannot be derived from sensory experience, then Hume's own explanation of cause and effect fails. The author asserts that the concept of causality is not simply the observation of two successive events; it is a third idea not given by the senses. The mind, not sensation, extracts this relation. Thus, Hume's reliance on experience cannot account for the notion of necessary connection. The argument is that the very concept of causality presupposes an a priori element, which Hume cannot deny without contradiction.

Key Terms
الضرورة
مفهوم العلية
الذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From sensing two successive events
B) From the mind extracting a non-empirical relation
C) From instinct and habit
D) From divine revelation

Key Takeaway
Causality is not a product of sensory experience but a rational concept that the mind extracts, as argued by al-Sadr in his critique of Hume.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a simple categorical syllogism using Islamic terms to show that the concept of causality cannot be derived from mere sensory experience. For example: 'Every concept of necessary connection is non-empirical. The concept of causality is a concept of necessary connection. Therefore, the concept of causality is non-empirical.' Then evaluate whether al-Sadr's conclusion follows.
?
Identify a passage from a kalam text (e.g., al-Sanusiyya) that relies on a necessary connection, and explain how al-Sadr's critique of Hume would defend that passage's epistemological basis.

Critique of Hume's Law of Association: Three Arguments
Author presents three arguments refuting Hume's law of association.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the three arguments against Hume's law of association as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
The law of association self-destructs: it cannot explain fixed principles or necessity.

Can all knowledge be reduced to sensory associations? David Hume argued that our ideas are connected by habit, not reason. The author here challenges that claim with three powerful objections. Let's walk through each argument and see how they undermine the law of association itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first argument: the dilemma
The author poses a choice: either the law of association is a primary rational truth, or it is not. If it is, then primary rational truths exist—contradicting empiricism. If it is not, then the law is not worth studying because it lacks fixed status.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
2
Extract the second argument: stability of concepts
The author points out that concepts like good and evil, right and wrong, beauty and ugliness, and mathematical principles have remained stable for centuries. If the law of association were the source of all thought, these would have changed constantly. Since they haven't, the law fails.
This appeals to historical evidence.
3
Unpack the third argument: necessity and causality
Hume himself uses the concept of necessity in his explanation of cause and effect. But necessity cannot be derived from sensory experience; it is a non-empirical relation. The mind, not the senses, creates the concept of causality. This shows that association alone is insufficient.
This demonstrates an internal inconsistency in Hume's position.
4
Summarise the conclusion
The author concludes that the law of association cannot explain the source of knowledge or its mechanism. Applied consistently, it would dissolve all rational laws, including itself. The critique thus highlights the need for a priori principles in epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(79) ... ۱ ) (ان) قانون تداعي المعاني نفسه هل هو حقيقة اولية عقلية ثابتة او قابلة للذوبان والتداعي في معان جديدة فاذا كان قانونا عقليا اوليا ثابتا فقد ثبت وجود القوانين الاولية واذا كان لم يتكامل حتى اليوم شكل القانون الاولي فهو ليس جديرا بالدراسة لانه لم يكتسب صفة ما ( ١ ) منذ ۲) ان هذا القانون يعني ان القوانين العلمية والمنطقية والاخلاقية والرياضية تتهشم كل حين لانها لاتمتلك صفة الثبات بسبب هذا التداعي المستمر للمعاني . وهذا غير حاصل لان مفاهيم مثل الخير والشر والخطأ والصواب والجمال والقبح او المباديء الرياضية لم يشملها قانون التداعي مئات خلت من السنين حتى يومنا هذا . مما يثبت ان التداعي في المعاني ليس اساس ولادة الفكر البشري ۳ ) (ان هيوم اعترف ضمنا بوجود علاقة غير تجريبية بين فكرتين وهي علاقة الضرورة. فان الضرورة سواء أكانت بين فكرتين أم بين واقعين موضوعيين لا يمكن اثباتها بالتجربة الحسية واذا كانت العلاقة مجرد مقارنة فلم يتحقق لهيوم ما اراد من تفسير عنصر الضرورة في قانون العلة والمعلول والحقيقة التي لا مجال لانكارها هي اننا نتصور مفهوم العلية سواء أصدقنا به ام لا وليس تصور العلية مركبا من تصور لشيئين متعاقبين بل هي فكرة ثالثة لم تدرك بالحس ولولا وجود العلية فمن اين ادرك هيوم هذه العلاقة الموضوعية بين الاشياء ؟ كيف تصور هيوم هذا القانون ؟ ان الذهن هو الذي انتزع العلاقة ولم تقدم الاحساسات أي معطيات في هذا الانتزاع ومجمل القول
۱ فلسفتنا محمد باقر الصدر، مصدر سابق ص ٦٧ ۲ فلسفتنا محمد باقر الصدر، مصدر سابق ص ۷۸ المصدر نفسه ص ٦٦
ان قانون تداعي المعاني لا يفسر مصدر المعرفة ولا آليتها ولو طبقنا هذا القانون فمعناه ذوبان جميع القوانين العقلية الاولية والعلمية والمنطقية والفلسفية بوجه يمنعها من ان تتخذ أي صفة لها ومنها نظرية تداعي المعاني
The law of association of meanings itself: Is it a primary rational fixed truth or susceptible to dissolution and association into new meanings? If it is a primary rational fixed law, then the existence of primary laws is proven. If it has not yet attained the form of a primary law, then it is not worthy of study because it has not acquired any quality. Secondly, this law means that scientific, logical, ethical, and mathematical laws shatter at every moment because they lack the property of stability due to this continuous association of meanings. But this does not happen because concepts like good and evil, wrong and right, beauty and ugliness, or mathematical principles have not been subject to the law of association for hundreds of years until today. This proves that association of meanings is not the basis for the birth of human thought. Thirdly, Hume implicitly acknowledged a non-empirical relation between two ideas, namely the relation of necessity. For necessity, whether between two ideas or between two objective realities, cannot be proven by sensory experience. If the relation were merely a comparison, then Hume would not have achieved what he wanted in explaining the element of necessity in the law of cause and effect. The undeniable truth is that we conceive the concept of causality whether we believe it or not. The concept of causality is not composed of the concept of two successive things; rather, it is a third idea not perceived by sense. Without causality, how did Hume perceive this objective relation between things? How did he conceive this law? It is the mind that extracted the relation, and sensations did not provide any data in this extraction. In summary, the law of association of meanings does not explain the source of knowledge nor its mechanism. If we apply this law, it would mean the dissolution of all primary rational, scientific, logical, and philosophical laws in a way that prevents them from taking any property, including the theory of association of meanings itself.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a critique of Hume's law of association. The author begins by posing a dilemma: either the law is a primary rational truth, in which case primary laws exist, or it is not, making it unworthy of study. The second argument shows that the law would undermine all stable concepts, but historical stability of ideas like good and evil disproves this. The third argument reveals that Hume implicitly relies on a non-empirical necessity relation; causality is a third concept not derived from sensation, proving that the mind, not the senses, extracts causal relations. The conclusion asserts that the law cannot explain knowledge and would lead to the dissolution of all rational and scientific principles.

Key Terms
قانون تداعي المعاني
العلية
الضرورة
التجربة الحسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the stability of concepts like good and evil disprove the law of association?
A) Because good and evil are subjective and change over time.
B) Because they have remained unchanged for centuries, contradicting the law's claim that meanings constantly shift.
C) Because the law itself is a primary rational truth.
D) Because Hume admitted they are not based on association.

Key Takeaway
Hume's law of association fails because it cannot account for fixed concepts, primary truths, or the necessary relation of causality; the mind actively produces concepts like causality independent of sensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Critique of the Active Intellect: Contradiction in Aristotle's Epistemology
Author introduces Aristotle's epistemology and criticizes the active intellect concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradiction between sensory and abstract knowledge in Aristotle's theory of the active intellect and reconstruct the author's critique.

Core Idea
Aristotle's active intellect contradicts his own empiricism: knowledge from senses yet from pure forms.

The author prefaces his critique by noting that philosophy concerns some causes and principles, and Aristotle's thought is confined to a rational perspective, so contradictions are expected. He then zeroes in on the concept of the active intellect, which he argues creates an obvious contradiction between sensory and abstract knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify Aristotle's two claims
Find where the passage states that Aristotle asserts all knowledge is connected to sensory knowledge, and separately that knowledge originates from intellectual forms (pure forms).
2
Locate the contradiction
The active intellect is a pure form, yet it mediates knowledge. This contradicts the empirical foundation that knowledge comes only from the senses.
3
Understand the author's framing
The author argues that this contradiction stems from Aristotle's purely rational perspective, which cannot escape such difficulties. Philosophy is limited to some causes and principles.
The author is setting up his critical approach.
4
Evaluate the critique's strength
Consider whether the author successfully demonstrates a logical inconsistency. The critique is valid if Aristotle indeed holds both positions without reconciliation.
This is a tool for analyzing philosophical doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينقل حالة الصور بالقوة إلى حالة الصور بالفعل أن العقل الفعال بمثابة النور الذي يضيء لنا المعقولات، وإن محور الارتكاز في المعرفة هو انتقال المعرفة بالقوة إلى المعرفة بالفعل. وبعبارة أوضح إن قوى النفس مقسمة على قسمين مدركة ومحركة. أما الذاكرة فهي عبارة عن الآثار التي يتركها الحس. وقد سجل الباحثون ثغرات وتناقضات مهمة على نظرية أرسطو، وقبل أن نعرض لهذه الطعونات نقول: لعل أحد تعاريف الفلسفة (هي العلم ببعض الأسباب وبعض المبادئ)، إن هذا القول ينطبق على نظرية أرسطو في المعرفة تمام الانطباق، لأن الفلاسفة يدرسون الصفات الظاهرية ويأخذون الموجود بما هو موجود، ولا يشذ الفيلسوف أرسطو عن هذه القاعدة، فإن تفكيره محصور في المنظور الفلسفي العقلي الصرف، لذلك مهما سمت الفلسفة وروادها لابد من وجود صعوبات جدية وتناقضات في نظرياتهم. وعلى صعيد نظرية القوة الكامنة نلاحظ الآتي: (1) إن الصعوبة الكبرى في نظرية القوة الكامنة هي مفهوم العقل الفعال، فإننا نجد أرسطو من ناحية يقول إن كل معرفة مرتبطة بمعرفة حسية، في حين نجده من ناحية أخرى يقول إن المعرفة صادرة عن صور عقلية وإن مصدر هذه الصور العقلية صور خالصة أي عارية عن كل مادة. فالعقل المنفعل والعقل الفعال هما صور خالصة أو ماهية تامة، وهذا خروج تام عن منطقة الحس. إن أرسطو كان عقليا أكثر بكثير جدا مما يتوهم في أول الأمر، وقد وقع في تناقض واضح بين الحسية الخالصة وبين العقلية المجردة.
He transfers the state of forms in potentiality to the state of forms in actuality, that the active intellect is like the light that illuminates the intelligibles for us. And that the pivot of knowledge is the transition of potential knowledge to actual knowledge. In clearer terms, the powers of the soul are divided into two parts: perceptive and motive. As for memory, it is the traces left by the senses. Researchers have recorded important gaps and contradictions in Aristotle's theory. Before we present these criticisms, we say: Perhaps one of the definitions of philosophy is 'it is the knowledge of some causes and some principles.' This statement applies perfectly to Aristotle's theory of knowledge, because philosophers study external qualities and take existence as it exists, and Aristotle is not an exception to this rule, for his thinking is confined to a purely rational philosophical perspective. Therefore, no matter how elevated philosophy and its pioneers, there must be serious difficulties and contradictions in their theories. Concerning the theory of potentiality, we note the following: (1) The greatest difficulty in the theory of potentiality is the concept of the active intellect. For we find Aristotle on one hand saying that all knowledge is connected to sensory knowledge, while on the other hand he says that knowledge originates from intellectual forms and that the source of these intellectual forms are pure forms, i.e., devoid of all matter. Thus the passive intellect and the active intellect are pure forms or complete essences, and this is a complete departure from the realm of the senses. Indeed, Aristotle was far more rational than is initially imagined, and he fell into an obvious contradiction between pure sensory and abstract intellectual.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage begins by summarizing Aristotle's theory of potential intellect: the active intellect is like light that brings potential forms to actuality. The author then notes that researchers have found gaps and contradictions. Before presenting criticisms, he offers a definition of philosophy as knowledge of some causes and principles, and argues that Aristotle's thought is confined to a rational perspective, so contradictions are inevitable. The first criticism is the contradiction between sensory knowledge and abstract forms: Aristotle claims all knowledge is from senses, but then says knowledge comes from pure forms. This is an obvious contradiction.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال (al-ʻaql al-faʻʻāl): The active intellect, described as the light that illuminates intelligibles and brings potential forms to actuality. [Note: In Aristotelian tradition, the active intellect is often considered separate from the individual soul; here it is critiqued as introducing a contradiction.]
العقل المنفعل (al-ʻaql al-munfaʻil): The passive intellect, which receives forms; together with the active intellect, they are considered pure forms without matter. [Note: No significant shift.]
القوة الكامنة (al-quwwa al-kāmina): Potentiality; the theory that forms exist potentially in matter and are actualized by the active intellect. [Note: In Aristotle, potentiality is a metaphysical concept; here it is applied specifically to epistemology.]
الصور الخالصة (al-ṣuwar al-khāliṣa): Pure forms, abstract and devoid of matter; the source of intellectual knowledge. [Note: No significant shift.]

Critique of Memory as a Productive Faculty
Critique of Aristotle's productive memory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the contradiction in Aristotle's definition of memory and explain why later Islamic psychologists separated memory from imagination.

Core Idea
Memory cannot be both a passive trace and an active producer of new images.

In this passage, the author identifies a second difficulty with Aristotle's theory of knowledge: his account of memory. While Aristotle initially defines memory as a mere trace left by the senses, he later assigns it an active, productive capacity. This inconsistency was noted by Alexander of Aphrodisias and would later influence Islamic faculty psychology.

Guided Path
1
Extract Aristotle's two definitions of memory
Find the two distinct statements about memory in the passage: one as 'traces left by the senses' and another as 'producing images by composing them.' Note how they differ.
2
Identify the contradiction
A faculty cannot be both purely receptive (storage) and productive (creating new forms) without additional justification. The passage says Aristotle 'added the attribute of action', which violates his own empiricist principle.
3
Appreciate the commentator's role
See how Alexander of Aphrodisias is cited as an ancient commentator who noticed this problem. This shows that critical analysis of philosophical theories has a long tradition.
4
Connect to Islamic psychology
Recognize that later Islamic philosophers like Ibn Sina addressed this by separating faculties: memory (al-dhākira) for storage, and imagination (al-mutakhayyila) for composition. This distinction avoids the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذاكرة هي الصعوبة الثانية: فهو يقول (الذاكرة هي عبارة عن الآثار التي يتركها الحس) بيد أن الشراح ولاسيما الإسكندر الأفروديسي يلاحظون أن أرسطو لم يجعل للذاكرة هذه الوظيفة السهلة فحسب التي لا تتجاوز الآثار الباقية من أشياء حسية، وإنما زاد عليها صفة الفعل كذلك، فجعل الذاكرة تفعل بمعنى تنتج صورا بأن يركبها بعضها مع بعض.
Memory is the second difficulty: He says 'Memory is the traces left by the senses,' but the commentators, especially Alexander of Aphrodisias, note that Aristotle did not assign to memory only this simple function of not exceeding the remaining traces of sensory things, but also added to it the attribute of action, making memory act in the sense of producing images by composing them with each other.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage identifies the second difficulty: Aristotle's concept of memory. Commentators observe that Aristotle made memory not merely a passive repository of sensory impressions, but an active faculty that can combine traces to produce new images. This contradicts his earlier claim that all knowledge comes from senses, because memory now generates something not directly given by sense.

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's account of memory is internally inconsistent: it cannot be both a passive trace and an active producer of images.

Aristotle's Excluded Sources of Knowledge
Author criticizes Aristotle for limiting knowledge to reason and senses, ignoring other sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which sources of knowledge Aristotle's epistemology excludes according to this critique and evaluate its validity using Islamic epistemological criteria.

Core Idea
Aristotle's epistemology is incomplete: it ignores fiṭra, dreams, and revelation.

The author continues his critique by highlighting that Aristotle's theory of knowledge, based solely on reason and senses, overlooks crucial sources recognized in Islamic thought. This lesson examines the fourth critique and its implications for defining knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
What does Aristotle limit knowledge to? Find the two sources mentioned: reason and senses.
The text says: 'حصر المعرفة بالعقل والحس فقط'
2
List the excluded sources
What additional sources does the author propose? Identify fiṭra, heredity, dreams, scriptures, and spiritual knowledge.
These are listed as: 'الفطرة الإنسانية والوراثة والرؤيا المنامية والكتب السماوية والمعارف الروحية الخاصة'
3
Evaluate the example
How does the dream example challenge Aristotle's theory? The dream of a neighbor's death coming true cannot be explained by reason or senses alone.
The author asks: 'هل تستطيع نظرية القوة الكامنة تفسيرها؟'
4
Assess the criticism
Is this a valid epistemological objection? Consider whether the dream example is a genuine counterexample to Aristotle's theory.
5
Connect to Islamic epistemology
How does this critique align with Islamic sources of knowledge? Islamic tradition accepts fiṭra, true dreams, and revelation as valid sources.
For example, the Qur'an and hadith affirm the validity of true dreams (ru'ya ṣādiqa).

The fourth criticism is that Aristotle's epistemology is limited to reason and sense, ignoring other sources of knowledge: human innate disposition (fiṭra), heredity, dreams, scriptures, and spiritual knowledge. The author gives the example of a veridical dream predicting a neighbor's death, which occurs frequently. Since the theory cannot explain such phenomena, it is inadequate. The author concludes by acknowledging the theory's merits but notes its narrowness and contradictions.

Critiquing Aristotle's Epistemology: Tools from Islamic Thought
Author criticizes Aristotle's theory of knowledge, memory, and potentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's criticism of Aristotle's theory of potentiality and evaluate its validity using Islamic epistemological criteria.

Core Idea
Aristotle's theory of knowledge is both too rational and too empirical, ignoring revelation and fitra.

This passage presents a modern critique of Aristotle's epistemology, focusing on his theory of potentiality and memory. The author argues that Aristotle's framework is narrow and contains internal contradictions. We will analyze this critique to sharpen our own ability to evaluate philosophical claims against Islamic sources of knowledge. By understanding the weaknesses in Aristotle's approach, we can better appreciate the comprehensive epistemology offered by Islam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main objections
Read the passage and list the specific points where the author finds fault with Aristotle's theory. The objections include: (1) contradiction between sensuality and rationalism, (2) memory being both passive and active, (3) failure to explain connection between body and pure forms, (4) exclusion of other sources of knowledge like fitra, dreams, revelation.
2
Examine the internal consistency of Aristotle's views
Focus on the memory example: Aristotle initially defines memory as traces of sense, but then gives it an active role. The author sees this as a contradiction. Check if this is a genuine inconsistency or a complementary function.
The comment of Alexander of Aphrodisias is used to support the critique.
3
Assess the author's alternative sources of knowledge
The author proposes that true knowledge includes not just reason and sense but also instinct (fitra), heredity, dream visions, heavenly books, and spiritual knowledge. Consider how these sources are validated in Islamic thought, e.g., the concept of 'ilm al-ladunni and prophetic dreams.
The example of a dream predicting a neighbor's death is given as empirical evidence.
4
Evaluate the critique's logical soundness
Determine whether the author's own argument is logically coherent. Does the author commit any fallacies, such as straw man or hasty generalization? Also, note that the author concedes the theory is 'one of the greatest' yet calls it narrow—is this a contradiction itself?
The critique is meant to refine, not reject entirely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعقل الفعال هما صور خالصة أو ماهية تامة وهذا خروج تام عن منطقة الحس إن أرسطو كان عقليا أكثر بكثير جدا مما يتوهم في أول الأمر وقد وقع في تناقض واضح بين الحسية الخالصة وبين العقلية المجردة. الذاكرة هي الصعوبة الثانية: فهو يقول الذاكرة هي عبارة عن الآثار التي يتركها الحس بيد أن الشراح ولاسيما الإسكندر الأفروديسي يلاحظون أن أرسطو لم يجعل للذاكرة هذه الوظيفة السهلة فحسب التي لا تتجاوز الآثار الباقية من أشياء حسية وإنما زاد عليها صفة الفعل كذلك فجعل الذاكرة تفعل بمعنى تنتج صورا بأن يركبها بعضها مع بعض. إن نظرية القوة الكامنة لم تشرح آلية الاتصال بين الجسم المادي وبين الصور الخالصة. إن أرسطو كان أسيرا لمهنته كفيلسوف فقد حصر المعرفة بالعقل والحس فقط من دون حساب لأهمية الفطرة الإنسانية والوراثة والرؤيا المنامية والكتب السماوية والمعارف الروحية الخاصة فمثلا لو أن شخصا رأى في المنام أن جاره قد مات وعندما استيقظ في الصباح وجد أن جاره قد مات فعلا. إن هذه الرؤيا وأمثالها تحصل مئات المرات فهل تستطيع نظرية القوة الكامنة تفسيرها؟ وخلاصة القول: إن نظرية القوة الكامنة تعد من أعظم نظريات الفلاسفة وأكثرها اقترابا من الحقيقة ولكنها أحادية ضيقة وفيها تناقضات صارخة.
The active intellect are pure forms or complete quiddity, and this is a complete departure from the realm of sense. Indeed, Aristotle was much more rational than is initially imagined. And he fell into a clear contradiction between pure sensuality and abstract rationality. Memory is the second difficulty: for he says that memory is the traces that sense leaves. But the commentators, especially Alexander of Aphrodisias, observe that Aristotle did not give memory this easy function only, which does not exceed the remaining traces of sensory things, but rather added to it the attribute of action as well, making memory act, meaning it produces images by composing some with others. The theory of potentiality did not explain the mechanism of connection between the material body and the pure forms. Aristotle was a captive of his profession as a philosopher, for he confined knowledge to reason and sense alone, without accounting for the importance of human instinct, heredity, dream vision, heavenly books, and special spiritual knowledge. For example, if a person saw in a dream that his neighbor had died, and when he woke up in the morning he found that his neighbor had indeed died. This vision and its like occur hundreds of times. Can the theory of potentiality explain them? In conclusion: the theory of potentiality is considered one of the greatest theories of the philosophers and the closest to the truth, but it is narrow and one-sided, and it contains glaring contradictions.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of Aristotle's epistemology, specifically his theory of knowledge and the concept of potentiality (al-quwwa al-kamina). The author begins by noting that Aristotle's rationalism is more extreme than commonly thought, and that he contradicts himself by oscillating between pure empiricism and abstract rationalism. The second point concerns memory: Aristotle initially defines memory as mere traces left by sense perception, but Alexander of Aphrodisias and other commentators note that Aristotle also gives memory an active role, composing images from those traces. This, the author implies, is inconsistent. Next, the author attacks the theory of potentiality, arguing that it fails to explain how the material body connects with pure, immaterial forms. Finally, the author criticizes Aristotle for restricting knowledge to reason and sense, ignoring other valid sources such as innate human nature (fitra), heredity, dreams, revelation, and spiritual insight. An example is given of a prophetic dream that accurately predicts a neighbor's death, which the theory of potentiality cannot account for. The conclusion acknowledges the greatness of the theory of potentiality but deems it ultimately narrow and contradictory. The commentary follows the author's sequential critique, explaining each objection in turn.

Key Terms
القوة الكامنة
الذاكرة
الفطرة
الرؤيا المنامية
المعارف الروحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is purely materialistic.

Key Takeaway
Aristotle's theory of potentiality, while influential, is narrow and contradictory; a comprehensive Islamic epistemology incorporates multiple sources beyond reason and sense.

Challenges of Extracting the Quranic Theory of Knowledge
Author introduces the Quran's extensive concern with knowledge and the challenges of extracting its theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the obstacles the author highlights in deducing a Quranic epistemology.

Core Idea
The Quran's integrated worldview makes extracting its theory of knowledge a perilous but rewarding task.

This passage introduces the Quran's comprehensive concern with knowledge: its sources, stages, methods, and controls. The author shares his methodology of studying the works of jurists and tafsir scholars, and defines tafsir as 'revealing the intelligible meaning.' He then warns that the Quran's vocabulary is so intertwined with its worldview and legislations that extracting a pure theory of knowledge is fraught with difficulties. Despite this, he attempts to formulate such a theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about the Quran's focus
The author states that the Quran pays 'great attention' to knowledge, covering its sources, stages, methods, and controls. This claim sets the foundation for the entire chapter.
Look for the Arabic phrase 'اهتماما بالغا' indicating strong emphasis.
2
Examine the definition of tafsir provided
The author defines tafsir as 'revealing the intelligible meaning' and notes it can apply to individual words and rare usages. This definition shows the scope of the interpretive tradition he draws upon.
Consider how this definition differs from more specific usages in Usul al-Fiqh.
3
Recognize the obstacles to extracting a theory
The author lists that Quranic vocabulary is mixed with its theory of life, the universe, and social, economic, and creedal legislations. This integration creates a 'path full of risks and obstacles.'
The Arabic phrase 'مليئا بالمخاطر والعقبات' underscores the challenge.
4
Understand the author's approach and humility
Despite the difficulties, the author 'attempted' to formulate the theory, acknowledging the complexity. His result is presented in the chapter that follows.
Note the author's tentative language ('حاولنا') showing scholarly caution.

لقد اهتم القرآن بالمعرفة ومصادرها ومراحلها وطرائقها وضوابطها اهتماما بالغا في ثنايا آياته وقد درسناها في خطوطها العامة بعد ملاحظة جهود فقهاء المسلمين في هذا المضمار وملاحظة كتب التفسير فسر : إظهار المعنى المعقول والتفسير قد يقال فيما يختص بمفردات الالفاظ وغريبها ١ وأول ما يلاحظه الباحث في نظرية القرآن الكريم هو ذلك الغنى المعرفي الهائل المتعدد الوجوه المفردات القرآن مما يجعل الولوج في لب القرآن لاستنباط او استخلاص نظريته في المعرفة طريق مليئا بالمخاطر والعقبات لان كلمات القرآن ممزوجة بنظريته في الحياة والكون وكم هائل من التشريعات الاجتماعية والاقتصادية والعقائدية وحاولنا وسط طريق شائك وصعب صياغة نظرية المعرفة في القرآن الكريم فتجلت على الصورة المرسومة بهذا الفصل. المصدر نفسه ص۳۸۱
The Quran has paid great attention to knowledge and its sources, stages, methods, and controls in the folds of its verses. We have studied it in its general outlines after observing the efforts of Muslim jurists in this field and observing the books of tafsir. Tafsir: revealing the intelligible meaning, and tafsir may be said concerning individual words and their rare usages. The first thing the researcher notices in the theory of the Noble Quran is that immense multifaceted cognitive richness of Quranic vocabulary, which makes entering the core of the Quran to deduce or extract its theory of knowledge a path full of risks and obstacles, because the words of the Quran are mixed with its theory of life and the universe and a vast amount of social, economic, and creedal legislations. We attempted, amidst a thorny and difficult path, to formulate the theory of knowledge in the Noble Quran, and it manifested in the form depicted in this chapter. Source same p. 381.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an introductory statement by the author about the Quran's attention to knowledge. The author begins by noting that the Quran has a comprehensive concern with knowledge—its sources, stages, methods, and controls. He states that he has studied these general outlines by observing the works of Muslim jurists and tafsir books. He then defines 'tafsir' as revealing the intelligible meaning, and notes that it can also refer to explaining individual words and rare usages. The author then highlights the immense richness of Quranic vocabulary, which makes extracting the Quran's epistemological theory challenging because the Quran's words are intertwined with its worldview and numerous legislations. Despite these difficulties, the author attempted to formulate this theory, and the result is presented in this chapter. The tone is humble and acknowledges the complexity of the task.
