The Senses in Islamic Epistemology
Author defines the senses, lists the five external and internal senses, and cites Quranic verses emphasizing hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five external senses and the internal senses as described in the Islamic epistemological tradition, and explain the Quranic emphasis on hearing and sight.

Core Idea
The senses are the gateway to all knowledge; losing one sense loses a whole domain of knowledge.

This passage from a coursebook on Islamic philosophy explains the role of the senses in knowledge. The author defines 'al-hawas' broadly, including both the well-known five external senses and internal sense organs. He then highlights the Quranic focus on hearing and sight as primary tools for learning, citing several verses to support the view that sensory perception is the foundation of human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of 'al-hawas'
Find how the author defines 'al-hawas' (senses). Note that it is the plural of 'hassa', and that people have traditionally counted them as five. The author introduces the concept of 'common sense' (al-hiss al-mushtarak) as a central station where all sensations meet.
The definition includes both external and internal senses.
2
List the five external senses
Identify the five external senses listed: the eye (sight), ear (hearing), nose (smell), skin (touch), and tongue (taste). Observe that each sense transmits a specific type of image from material reality to the brain.
These are the classical five senses in the Islamic tradition.
3
Recognize internal senses and feelings
Look for the author's examples of internal sense organs: stomach (hunger, thirst), lungs (suffocation), skin (pain, heat, cold), and also emotions like joy and sadness. The author includes these under the term 'sense'.
This broadens the definition beyond the typical five.
4
Analyze the Quranic verses
Read the verses cited: Maryam 98, Yusuf 87, Al Imran 52, Anbiya 12, and Nahl 78. Notice the root 'h-s-s' and the specific emphasis on hearing (sam') and sight (basar) in Nahl 78.
The Quran uses 'sense' as a verb and highlights hearing and sight.
5
Synthesize the epistemological role
Understand the author's conclusion: at birth, humans lack knowledge and have only a readiness to learn. Sensory perceptions (partial perceptions) prepare the human for imaginative and intellectual knowledge.
This aligns with the Islamic view that knowledge starts from sensory experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ - دور الحواس في المعرفة
الحواس : جمع حاسة وقد حسبها الناس خمسة يجتمع منتوجها كحس مشترك أشبه بالمركز العام الذي تتلاقى فيه جميع الاحساسات ولكل حاسة اساس فيه فالحس كرمز لبقية الحواس ينقل صورة من الواقع المادي الى الدماغ . العين تنقل صورة الجسم . الاذن تنقل صورة الصوت . الانف تنقل صورة الرائحة . الجلد ينقل صورة الشيء الملموس . اللسان ينقل صورة الشيء ء المذاق. وفي الحقيقة ان الحواس لاتقتصر على الحواس الخمسة المذكورة آنفا فهناك اعضاء الاحساس الداخلي فالمعدة مثلا تشعر الانسان بالجوع والعطش والرئتين تشعر الانسان بالاختناق والجلد يشعر الانسان بالالم والحرارة والبرودة وهناك احاسيس بالفرح او الحزن . وقد اجمع الفقهاء والفلاسفة على تسمية ماتنقله هذه الاعضاء من الانطباعات البيئية والمنبهات المختلفة الميكانيكية والكيميائية والحرارية والضوئية بكلمة الحس وركزوا على الحواس الخارجية الخمسة وهي اللمس والذوق والبصر والسمع والشم التي تمنحنا تصورات من عالم الطبيعة حتى اشتهر بين جمهور الحكماء القول المأثور: ان من فقد حسا فقد علما . وقد اهتم القرآن الكريم بتاشير اهمية الحس اهتماما واضحا وسنذكر هنا بعض الآيات الكريمة حول مفردة الحس قال تعالى (هَلْ تُحِس مِنْهُم مِنْ أَحَدٍ أو تَسْمَعَ لَهُمْ رِكْزًا ) () (فَتَحَسْسُوا مِنْ يُوسُفَ وَأَخِيهِ )٢) فَلَمَّا أَحَس عِيسَى مِنهُمُ الْكُفْرَ ) ۳) (فَلَمَّا أَحَسُوا بَأْسَنَا إِذَا هُمْ منها يركُضُونَ)، وعلى صعيد دور الحواس الخمسة الظاهرة في صناعة المعرفة البشرية نجد في آيات القرآن الكريم تركيزا بينا على اهمية حاستي السمع والبصر . قال تعالى ( وَاللهُ أَخْرَجَكُمْ مِنْ بُطُونِ أُمِّهَاتِكُمْ لَا تَعْلَمُونَ شَيْئًا وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ السَّمْعَ وَالْأَبْصَارَ وَالْأَفْئِدَةَ لَعَلَّكُمْ تَشْكُرُونَ) يتبين من سياق الآيات الكريمة ( إن نفس الانسان أول ما يولد تكون خالية من أية معرفة ولا تمتلك الا استعدادا للتعلم فيأتي دور الادراكات الجزئية الحسية التي هي قسم من المعرفة التي يحتاج اليها الانسان في نفسها فتعده للمعرفة الخيالية وتعده للمعرفة العقلية ( ٦ فالانسان عندما خرج من بطن امه وظهر الى هذا العالم كان
2 - The role of the senses in knowledge
Senses: plural of sense; people have counted them as five. Their product gathers as a common sense, like a central station where all sensations meet, and every sense has a basis in it. The sense, as a symbol for the rest of the senses, transmits an image from material reality to the brain. The eye transmits an image of the body. The ear transmits an image of sound. The nose transmits an image of smell. The skin transmits an image of the touched thing. The tongue transmits an image of the tasted thing. In reality, the senses are not limited to the aforementioned five; there are internal sense organs. For example, the stomach makes a person feel hunger and thirst; the lungs make a person feel suffocation; the skin makes a person feel pain, heat, and cold; and there are feelings of joy or sadness. The jurists and philosophers have agreed to call what these organs transmit of environmental impressions and various mechanical, chemical, thermal, and optical stimuli by the word 'sense', and they focused on the five external senses: touch, taste, sight, hearing, and smell, which grant us perceptions from the natural world, until the famous saying among the majority of sages became widespread: 'He who loses a sense loses knowledge.' The Noble Quran has clearly emphasized the importance of sense, and we will mention here some noble verses concerning the term 'sense'. He said, exalted: 'Do you sense any of them or hear a whisper?' [Maryam 98], 'So sense [literally: seek sensing] Yusuf and his brother' [Yusuf 87], 'When Isa sensed from them disbelief' [Al Imran 52], 'And when they sensed Our might, behold they fled from it' [Anbiya 12]. And on the level of the role of the five apparent senses in the production of human knowledge, we find in the verses of the Noble Quran a clear focus on the importance of the two senses of hearing and sight. He said, exalted: 'And Allah brought you out of the wombs of your mothers not knowing anything, and He made for you hearing, sight, and hearts that you might give thanks' [Nahl 78]. It becomes clear from the context of the noble verses that the human soul at birth is devoid of any knowledge and possesses only a readiness to learn; then comes the role of partial sensory perceptions, which are a part of the knowledge that the human needs in itself, and they prepare him for imaginative knowledge and prepare him for intellectual knowledge. So when a person comes out of his mother's womb and appears into this world, he was...
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about the senses. The author begins by defining 'al-hawas' (senses) as the plural of 'hassa', and states that people have traditionally counted them as five. He explains that the products of these senses converge in a 'common sense' (hiss mushtarak), which acts as a central station where all sensations meet. Each sense has a basis in this common sense. The sense, as a representative of the other senses, transmits an image from material reality to the brain. The author then lists the five external senses: eye (sight), ear (hearing), nose (smell), skin (touch), and tongue (taste). However, he immediately clarifies that the senses are not limited to these five; there are internal sense organs. He gives examples: the stomach senses hunger and thirst, the lungs sense suffocation, the skin senses pain, heat, and cold, and there are also feelings of joy or sadness. The author notes that jurists and philosophers have agreed to call the transmissions of these organs—environmental impressions and various stimuli—by the word 'sense', but they focused on the five external senses: touch, taste, sight, hearing, and smell. These five grant perceptions from the natural world, leading to the famous saying among sages: 'He who loses a sense loses knowledge.' The author then turns to the Quran's emphasis on the senses, noting that the Quran has clearly stressed the importance of sense. He cites several verses that use the root 'h-s-s' (to sense, to perceive). Then he specifically highlights the emphasis on hearing and sight in Quranic verses, quoting the famous verse from Surah An-Nahl (16:78) which mentions hearing, sight, and hearts. From this, he concludes that at birth a human is without knowledge and only has a readiness to learn; then partial sensory perceptions play a role in preparing the human for imaginative and intellectual knowledge. The passage ends with a partial sentence suggesting the human's entry into the world. The author logically moves from a definition of senses, to a discussion of their types, to their epistemological role, and finally to Quranic support.

Key Terms
الحواس
الحس المشترك
الحواس الخارجية

Key Takeaway
The senses, both external and internal, are the foundation of all human knowledge, as affirmed by the Quranic emphasis on hearing and sight in the process of learning.

Hearing as the Superior Sense in Knowledge Acquisition
Author argues for the superiority of hearing over sight using historical examples and Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the author's argument for the superiority of hearing over sight using historical and Quranic evidence, and evaluate its epistemological implications.

Core Idea
Hearing remains vigilant during sleep, making it the primary gateway to knowledge.

In this lesson, we examine a unique argument from the Islamic philosophical tradition that elevates the sense of hearing above sight. The author combines empirical observation (no deaf geniuses in history) with a Quranic verse to make his case. We will dissect his reasoning and consider its place in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
Find the sentence where the author states that hearing remains vigilant during sleep while other senses sleep. This is the core claim.
2
Examine the historical evidence
The author claims that no deaf person has been a genius in history, while many blind persons have been scholars and giants in philosophy, arts, and literature. List the examples given: Abu al-Ala al-Ma'arri, Bashar ibn Burd, Taha Husayn.
3
Analyze the Quranic verse
The author quotes Quran 22:46: 'It is not the eyes that are blind, but the hearts in the breasts are blind.' Reflect on how this verse is used to show that the Quran does not disparage the blind but rather emphasizes spiritual insight.
4
Evaluate the argument's strength
Consider whether the historical observation and the Quranic verse genuinely support the superiority of hearing. Is the historical claim verifiable? Does the Quranic verse directly address the superiority of hearing? This step develops critical thinking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفقد علاقته بالكون كما يقول بعض الحكماء وعندما ينام الانسان في الليل فان كل حواسه تنام الا حاسة السمع فأنها تبقى دائما متيقظة ولو قلبنا صفحات التاريخ القديم والحديث لم نجد من بين العلماء او النابغين احدا منهم أصم في حين نجد من بين فاقدي البصر علماء وعمالقة في الفلسفة والفنون والآداب كأبي العلاء المعري وبشار بن برد الشاعر والاديب العربي طه حسين. وفي حين نجد الاستصغار للانسان الاصم في القرآن الكريم نجده لم يقل في الاعمى الا تلطيفا وتشجيعا قال (لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَارُ وَلَكِنْ تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ)
It loses its relationship with the universe, as some sages say. When a person sleeps at night, all his senses sleep except for the sense of hearing, for it always remains vigilant. If we turn over the pages of ancient and modern history, we do not find among scientists or geniuses anyone who is deaf, whereas we find among the blind scientists and giants in philosophy, arts, and literature, such as Abu al-Ala al-Ma'arri, Bashar ibn Burd, the poet and Arab writer Taha Husayn. While we find disdain for the deaf man in the Holy Quran, we do not find regarding the blind except leniency and encouragement. He said: 'It is not the eyes that are blind, but the hearts in the breasts are blind.'
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological reflection on the senses. The author begins by quoting sages that the sense of hearing remains awake during sleep, unlike other senses. He then supports this by observing that history shows no deaf genius, while many blind scholars achieved greatness. He contrasts this with the Quranic attitude: the Quran does not disparage the blind but rather elevates their insight. The verse 'It is not the eyes that are blind, but the hearts in the breasts are blind' (Quran 22:46) is cited to show that true blindness is spiritual. The passage thus establishes the unique status of hearing in knowledge acquisition.

Traditional Definition of Intellect as Brain
Author defines the intellect as the brain and describes its structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the traditional identification of the intellect ('aql) with the brain and evaluate its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
The traditional definition of 'aql as the brain is a physicalist reduction, contrasting with the immaterial concept in Islamic philosophy.

In this passage, the author presents a traditional definition that equates the intellect with the brain – a physical organ. This definition describes the brain's anatomy and argues that nerve cell stability ensures personality consistency. However, this material identification conflicts with the predominant Islamic philosophical view of 'aql as an immaterial substance capable of abstract thought. Understanding this contrast is crucial for discussions on the nature of knowledge and the soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Locate the term being defined (العقل) and what it is defined as (الدماغ). Note the definition's structure.
The definiendum is 'aql, the definiens is the brain with its parts and functions.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
Identify the broader category (genus) and the distinguishing characteristic (differentia). Here, the genus is 'organ located under the skull' and the differentia includes its composition (cerebrum, cerebellum) and its functions (perceiver of sciences, storage place).
Consider whether this fits the classical hadd or rasm pattern.
3
Compare with Islamic philosophical definitions
Recall that in Islamic philosophy (e.g., Ibn Sina, al-Ghazali), 'aql is an immaterial substance that abstracts universals. Contrast this physical definition with that metaphysical view.
The shift alert indicates this passage uses a non-standard definition.
4
Evaluate the implications for epistemology
If 'aql is merely the brain, how can it grasp abstract, non-material concepts like the divine attributes? This tension highlights the need for a more nuanced understanding.
This step connects to the 'where_this_tool_lives' application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن التعريفات التقليدية ان العقل هو الدماغ الذي يقع تحت الجمجمة من الرأس ويتكون من جزأين رئيسين هما المخ والمخيخ الذي يقع اسفل المخ. وينقسم المخ الى جزأين رئيسين هما الفص الأيمن والفص الأيسر وهو المدرك للعلوم والامور المادية وهو ايضا مكان تخزين المعلومات والمدارك الحسية واللفظية والمعنوية والخبرات المكتسبة والفطرية ) ٢ (لقد ثبت في الطب الحديث ان خلايا الانسان في الجلد والعضلات والعظام والعيون تتجدد كل سبع سنوات مرة واحدة ماعدا الخلايا العصبية فانها تتوقف عن النمو عند السنة السابعة تقريبا حيث ان ۱۰/۹ من المخ ينمو في تلك الفترة . ولو تغيرت الخلايا العصبية لتغيرت شخصية الانسان ولكان له عدة تصرفات في يوم واحد. ان دماغ الانسان عند بلوغه أو هرمه هو ذاته عند طفولته وان ما يتطور هو التفكير والحجم) ٣
Among the traditional definitions is that the intellect is the brain which is located under the skull from the head and consists of two main parts: the cerebrum and the cerebellum which is below the cerebrum. The cerebrum is divided into two main parts: the right lobe and the left lobe, and it is the perceiver of sciences and material matters, and it is also the storage place of information, sensory, verbal, and conceptual perceptions, and acquired and innate experiences. (2) It has been established in modern medicine that human cells in the skin, muscles, bones, and eyes regenerate once every seven years, except for nerve cells, which stop growing at approximately the seventh year, as 9/10 of the brain grows in that period. If nerve cells changed, the person's personality would change, and he would have multiple behaviors in one day. The human brain at maturity or old age is the same as at childhood, and what develops is thinking and size. (3)
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents a traditional definition of the intellect ('aql) by identifying it with the brain. The author describes the brain's location under the skull, its two main parts (cerebrum and cerebellum), and the division of the cerebrum into right and left lobes. It states that the brain is the organ that perceives sciences and material matters, and stores various types of perceptions and experiences. Then the author cites a modern medical fact that most human cells regenerate every seven years, but nerve cells stop growing around age seven, and 9/10 of the brain develops in that period. The author argues that if nerve cells changed, personality would be unstable. Instead, the brain remains structurally the same from childhood to old age; only thinking and size develop. This definition focuses on the physical aspect of the intellect, contrasting with the later Quranic perspective that emphasizes the act of reasoning.

الدماغ

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the main reason nerve cells do not regenerate?
A) They stop growing after age seven to maintain personality stability.
B) They regenerate every seven years like other cells.
C) They are not affected by aging.
D) They only grow during childhood and then die.

Key Takeaway
The traditional definition of 'aql as the brain illustrates a physicalist approach, but Islamic philosophy typically regards 'aql as an immaterial, God-given faculty for grasping universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition of 'aql presented here. Then compare with the definition of 'aql in al-Ghazali's Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din, where he discusses the intellectual faculty as a light from Allah.
?
Construct a counterargument: If the intellect is only the brain, how would you explain abstract reasoning about non-material entities (e.g., the divine attributes)? Use the distinction between physical and metaphysical.

The Intellect vs. the Senses: A Productive Faculty
Contrast between senses and intellect with tree analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sensory knowledge and intellectual knowledge using the tree analogy, and identify an example from Islamic theology where the intellect generates new knowledge beyond the senses.

Core Idea
The intellect transforms sensory data into new knowledge like a tree turns raw elements into fruit.

Before diving into the six mental operations, the author establishes a foundational distinction: the intellect is not a passive receiver like the senses—it actively produces new knowledge. The tree analogy brilliantly captures this. In this lesson, you will unpack that analogy and see how it applies to Islamic epistemology, for instance in how the intellect derives the existence of the Creator from the contingent world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claims about the intellect
Read the opening lines: the intellect discriminates between opposites (good/evil, truth/falsehood, etc.) and generates new knowledge. Contrast this with the senses, which only transmit images without revealing anything beyond.
Notice that the author lists 14 pairs of opposites—this shows the intellect's scope.
2
Analyze the tree analogy
The intellect is like a tree drawing water, gases, and sun from nature (sensory input) and producing fruit (new knowledge). What are the raw materials? What is the fruit? How does the production happen?
The analogy emphasizes transformation, not just combination.
3
Apply to an Islamic theological example
Consider the proof of God's existence from contingency: the senses perceive a changing world; the intellect infers a Necessary Being beyond perception. Here, the intellect produces knowledge (God's existence) not directly given by the senses.
This example shows the intellect's productive role in Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العقل هو مركز التمييز بين الخير والشر ، الجميل والقبيح ، الخبيث والطيب ، النافع والضار ، الحق والباطل ، الفجور والتقوى ، السرور والحزن ، الحب والبغض ، الفضيلة والرذيلة ، العدل والظلم ، الصواب والخطأ، الحياء والفحش ، النجاسة والطهارة . وجميع النقائض والمترادفات والقرائن . ان العقل وان كان يستمد كثيرا من معارفه من الحواس الا انه يولد أيضا معارف جديدة ويمكن تشبيهه بالشجر الذي يستمد الماء والغازات والشمس من الطبيعة وينتج الثمر، ويتميز عمل العقل عن عمل الحس ان الحس لا يتعدى المحسوسات فهو ينقل صورا عنها من دون أن يكشف عن شيء آخر سوى ما تعلق به أما العقل فالامر فيه مختلف فانه ينتقل من ادراكات الى اخرى بعمليات عدة يمكن اجمال اهمها بالآتي: -۱ الاستنتاج المراد من الاستنتاج استخراج حكم موضوع مشخص من حكم كلي مستنبط . وهذا اسمى عمليات العقل في مجال المعارف . -۲- إدراك المفاهيم الكلية : يقول صدر المتالهين الشيرازي : ان درك الكلية ليست الا تبديل المعرفة الحسية بالمعرفة العقلية وحاصلها ان للمعرفة مراحل ثلاث ؛ أ - مرحلة الاحساس ب_ مرحلة الحفظ ج مرحلة إدراك مفهوم كلي _ مثال ؛ إذا اطل الانسان على منظر بهي رائع جميل فالصورة الواردة من المبصر الى الذهن تسمى صورة حسية عندما يبتعد الانسان عن ذلك المنظر يبقى في الذهن اثره في النفس وهذا هو الصورة الخيالية وعندما يريد الانسان ان يستحضر في ذهنه المناظر الطبيعية يجمع بين تلك الصور الخيالية ويعطي مفهوما كليا . ٣ - تصنيف الموجودات : البخل والحسد والعشق والمحبة والالوان والاشكال ان هذه المسميات والحالات هي من ضمن البصائر التي سنتطرق اليها في الفصل الآتي . ٤ - التجزئة والتحليل : أي تجزئة مفهوم واحد الى مفاهيم كثيرة كتحليل الحيوانات الى ناطق وصامت وتحليل الطيور الى مهاجر ومستقر وتحليل الحجم الى طول وعرض وارتفاع . ه - التركيب : التركيب حين يقوم العقل بتركيب قضيتين ويستنتج منهما قضية ثالثة بنتيجة قاطعة . كقضية اذا وضعت النار تحت قدر الماء ارتفعت درجة حرارة الماء . ٦ - درك المفاهيم الابداعية: كمفهوم الرئيس والمرءوس ومفهوم الاجارة والمجير في الاعتبارات الاجتماعية وغيرها من المفاهيم الاعتبارية في تفرعات الحياة المجتمع البشري.
The intellect is the center of discrimination between good and evil, beautiful and ugly, bad and good, beneficial and harmful, truth and falsehood, immorality and piety, joy and sadness, love and hate, virtue and vice, justice and injustice, correctness and error, modesty and indecency, impurity and purity. And all opposites, synonyms, and indications. Although the intellect derives much of its knowledge from the senses, it also generates new knowledge. It can be likened to a tree that draws water, gases, and sun from nature and produces fruit. The work of the intellect is distinguished from the work of the senses in that the senses do not go beyond the sensible; they transmit images of them without revealing anything other than what they are attached to. As for the intellect, the matter is different, for it moves from perceptions to others through several operations, the most important of which can be summarized as follows: 1- Inference: the aim of inference is to extract a specific ruling on a specific subject from a derived universal ruling. This is the noblest of the intellect's operations in the field of knowledge. 2- Perception of universal concepts: Sadr al-Muta'allihin al-Shirazi says: 'Indeed, the perception of universality is nothing but the transformation of sensory knowledge into intellectual knowledge.' Its outcome is that knowledge has three stages: a- the stage of sensation; b- the stage of retention; c- the stage of perceiving a universal concept. Example: When a person looks at a beautiful, splendid scene, the image arriving from the seen object to the mind is called a sensory image. When the person moves away from that scene, its effect remains in the mind and in the soul, and this is the imaginary image. When the person wants to bring to mind the natural scenes, he combines those imaginary images and produces a universal concept. 3- Classification of existents: stinginess, envy, love, affection, colors, shapes—these names and states are among the insights that we will discuss in the following chapter. 4- Part-whole analysis: i.e., analyzing one concept into many concepts, like analyzing animals into rational and non-rational, analyzing birds into migratory and resident, analyzing volume into length, width, and height. 5- Synthesis: synthesis when the intellect combines two propositions and deduces from them a third proposition with a decisive result, such as the proposition: 'If fire is placed under a pot of water, the temperature of the water rises.' 6- Perception of creative concepts: such as the concept of leader and subordinate, the concept of lease and lessor in social considerations, and other conventional concepts in the branches of human societal life.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition of the functions of the human intellect (al-‘aql). It begins by asserting that the intellect is the faculty that discriminates between a wide range of opposites—moral, aesthetic, practical, and metaphysical. It then acknowledges that while the intellect draws much from the senses, it is productive, generating new knowledge not directly given by sensation. The analogy of a tree is used: just as a tree transforms raw elements into fruit, the intellect transforms sensory data into higher knowledge. The distinction between sense and intellect is clarified: the senses only transmit images of their objects, whereas the intellect moves from one perception to another through specific operations. Six such operations are enumerated: (1) Inference (istintāj): extracting a particular judgment from a universal judgment. This is considered the highest intellectual operation. For example, in Islamic jurisprudence, from the universal rule 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' one infers that a specific beverage containing alcohol is forbidden. (2) Perception of universal concepts (idrāk al-mafāhīm al-kulliyya): quoting Mulla Sadra, the author explains that knowledge passes through three stages: sensory, imaginal (khayālī), and conceptual. When one sees a beautiful landscape, the initial image is sensory; after leaving, the retained image is imaginary; when combining multiple such images, a universal concept (e.g., 'beauty') is formed. (3) Classification (taṣnīf): the mind groups existents according to shared attributes like stinginess, colors, etc. (4) Part-whole analysis (tajzi’a wa al-taḥlīl): breaking a single concept into its constituent concepts, e.g., analyzing 'animal' into 'rational' and 'non-rational,' or analyzing a physical volume into length, width, and height. (5) Synthesis (tarkīb): the mind combines two propositions to deduce a third, as in a syllogism. The example given is a causal statement: 'If fire is placed under a pot of water, the temperature rises.' In Islamic logic, this resembles a conditional syllogism. (6) Perception of creative concepts (idrāk al-mafāhīm al-ibdā‘iyya): these are conventional concepts like 'leader' and 'subordinate,' or 'lease' and 'lessor,' which are not found in external reality but are constructed by the mind for social organization. Throughout, the author emphasizes that the mind does not merely passively receive but actively processes and produces knowledge.

Key Terms

إدراك المفاهيم الكلية
التجزئة والتحليل
التركيب
المفاهيم الابداعية

A) By passively receiving sensory images
B) By transforming sensory data through its own operations
C) By memorizing revelation
D) By discarding sensory information

Key Takeaway
The intellect is not a passive mirror of the senses; it actively transforms sensory input into new, higher knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Give an example from your own understanding where the intellect produces knowledge that the senses alone cannot provide. Use the tree analogy to explain the transformation.
?
Identify a verse from the Qur'an that calls upon the intellect (tafakkur, tadabbur) and explain how it relates to the productive nature of the intellect.

Inference (Istintāj): The Noblest Mental Operation
Definition and importance of inference as the noblest operation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid inference (istintāj) by applying a universal ruling from Islamic jurisprudence to a specific case.

Core Idea
Inference extracts a specific judgment from a universal, making it the pinnacle of intellectual operations.

Among the six operations, inference (istintāj) is given the highest rank. It is the process of deriving a particular judgment from a universal one—essentially the application of a general rule to a specific instance. This is the workhorse of Islamic jurisprudence (fiqh), where a mujtahid takes a universal principle from the Qur'an or Sunnah and applies it to a new case. In this lesson, you will learn how to perform this operation correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal premise
Find a universal ruling from a sacred text or derived principle. For example, the Qur'anic verse: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (khamr is haram). This is a universal statement covering all intoxicants.
Universal means it applies to all individuals of a category.
2
Identify the specific subject
Take a particular substance, e.g., nabidh (date wine) in a specific case. Determine if it falls under the universal category of intoxicants.
The specific subject must be clearly defined.
3
Extract the specific judgment
Apply the universal ruling to the specific subject: if nabidh is an intoxicant, then it is forbidden. The conclusion is: 'This nabidh is forbidden.'
The inference is valid only if the specific subject genuinely falls under the universal category.
4
Verify the inference's validity
Check that the universal premise is sound (from revealed or consensus source) and that the specific case is correctly subsumed. If both conditions hold, the inference is valid.
In Islamic logic, this is a type of immediate inference (taṭbīq), but the author treats it as a distinct operation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Perception of Universal Concepts: From Sensation to Abstraction
Explanation of the three-stage process of universal concept formation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the three-stage process (sensation, retention, conceptual unification) that transforms sensory experience into a universal concept, using the example of a beautiful landscape.

Core Idea
Universal concepts are the fruit of a three-stage transformation of sensory images into abstract ideas.

The perception of universal concepts (idrāk al-mafāhīm al-kulliyya) is the operation through which the mind moves from particular sensory experiences to general ideas. The author quotes Mulla Sadra, who identifies three stages: sensation (al-ḥiss), retention (al-ḥifẓ / khayāl), and conceptual unification (idrāk ma‘nā kullī). This is how we form concepts like 'beauty,' 'justice,' or 'humanity' from multiple encounters. In this lesson, you will unpack this process and see its relevance to how we understand abstract terms in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three stages in Mulla Sadra's model
Read the author's account: stage a (sensation) – the raw sensory image; stage b (retention/imaginal) – the image remains after the object is gone; stage c (universal concept) – the mind combines multiple retained images into a single abstract concept.
This is a psychological account of concept formation.
2
Analyze the example of a beautiful landscape
The author gives an example: seeing a beautiful scene produces a sensory image; after leaving, the memory (imaginal image) remains; later, recalling multiple such scenes yields a universal concept of 'beauty' itself.
The universal concept is not a mere sum of images but a new mental entity.
3
Apply to an Islamic concept
Take the concept of 'justice' (ʿadl). From seeing fair judgments in different contexts, the mind forms a universal concept of justice. Similarly, from reading multiple verses about God's justice, a person forms a theological understanding.
This shows why abstract concepts in Islam are grounded in sensory and experiential data.

العقل هو مركز التمييز بين الخير والشر ، الجميل والقبيح ، الخبيث والطيب ، النافع والضار ، الحق والباطل ، الفجور والتقوى ، السرور والحزن ، الحب والبغض ، الفضيلة والرذيلة ، العدل والظلم ، الصواب والخطأ، الحياء والفحش ، النجاسة والطهارة . وجميع النقائض والمترادفات والقرائن . ان العقل وان كان يستمد كثيرا من معارفه من الحواس الا انه يولد أيضا معارف جديدة ويمكن تشبيهه بالشجر الذي يستمد الماء والغازات والشمس من الطبيعة وينتج الثمر، ويتميز عمل العقل عن عمل الحس ان الحس لا يتعدى المحسوسات فهو ينقل صورا عنها من دون أن يكشف عن شيء آخر سوى ما تعلق به أما العقل فالامر فيه مختلف فانه ينتقل من ادراكات الى اخرى بعمليات عدة يمكن اجمال اهمها بالآتي: -۱ الاستنتاج المراد من الاستنتاج استخراج حكم موضوع مشخص من حكم كلي مستنبط . وهذا اسمى عمليات العقل في مجال المعارف . -۲- إدراك المفاهيم الكلية : يقول صدر المتالهين الشيرازي : ان درك الكلية ليست الا تبديل المعرفة الحسية بالمعرفة العقلية وحاصلها ان للمعرفة مراحل ثلاث ؛ أ - مرحلة الاحساس ب_ مرحلة الحفظ ج مرحلة إدراك مفهوم كلي _ مثال ؛ إذا اطل الانسان على منظر بهي رائع جميل فالصورة الواردة من المبصر الى الذهن تسمى صورة حسية عندما يبتعد الانسان عن ذلك المنظر يبقى في الذهن اثره في النفس وهذا هو الصورة الخيالية وعندما يريد الانسان ان يستحضر في ذهنه المناظر الطبيعية يجمع بين تلك الصور الخيالية ويعطي مفهوما كليا . ٣ - تصنيف الموجودات : البخل والحسد والعشق والمحبة والالوان والاشكال ان هذه المسميات والحالات هي من ضمن البصائر التي سنتطرق اليها في الفصل الآتي . ٤ - التجزئة والتحليل : أي تجزئة مفهوم واحد الى مفاهيم كثيرة كتحليل الحيوانات الى ناطق وصامت وتحليل الطيور الى مهاجر ومستقر وتحليل الحجم الى طول وعرض وارتفاع . ه - التركيب : التركيب حين يقوم العقل بتركيب قضيتين ويستنتج منهما قضية ثالثة بنتيجة قاطعة . كقضية اذا وضعت النار تحت قدر الماء ارتفعت درجة حرارة الماء . ٦ - درك المفاهيم الابداعية: كمفهوم الرئيس والمرءوس ومفهوم الاجارة والمجير في الاعتبارات الاجتماعية وغيرها من المفاهيم الاعتبارية في تفرعات الحياة المجتمع البشري.
The intellect is the center of discrimination between good and evil, beautiful and ugly, bad and good, beneficial and harmful, truth and falsehood, immorality and piety, joy and sadness, love and hate, virtue and vice, justice and injustice, correctness and error, modesty and indecency, impurity and purity. And all opposites, synonyms, and indications. Although the intellect derives much of its knowledge from the senses, it also generates new knowledge. It can be likened to a tree that draws water, gases, and sun from nature and produces fruit. The work of the intellect is distinguished from the work of the senses in that the senses do not go beyond the sensible; they transmit images of them without revealing anything other than what they are attached to. As for the intellect, the matter is different, for it moves from perceptions to others through several operations, the most important of which can be summarized as follows: 1- Inference: the aim of inference is to extract a specific ruling on a specific subject from a derived universal ruling. This is the noblest of the intellect's operations in the field of knowledge. 2- Perception of universal concepts: Sadr al-Muta'allihin al-Shirazi says: 'Indeed, the perception of universality is nothing but the transformation of sensory knowledge into intellectual knowledge.' Its outcome is that knowledge has three stages: a- the stage of sensation; b- the stage of retention; c- the stage of perceiving a universal concept. Example: When a person looks at a beautiful, splendid scene, the image arriving from the seen object to the mind is called a sensory image. When the person moves away from that scene, its effect remains in the mind and in the soul, and this is the imaginary image. When the person wants to bring to mind the natural scenes, he combines those imaginary images and produces a universal concept. 3- Classification of existents: stinginess, envy, love, affection, colors, shapes—these names and states are among the insights that we will discuss in the following chapter. 4- Part-whole analysis: i.e., analyzing one concept into many concepts, like analyzing animals into rational and non-rational, analyzing birds into migratory and resident, analyzing volume into length, width, and height. 5- Synthesis: synthesis when the intellect combines two propositions and deduces from them a third proposition with a decisive result, such as the proposition: 'If fire is placed under a pot of water, the temperature of the water rises.' 6- Perception of creative concepts: such as the concept of leader and subordinate, the concept of lease and lessor in social considerations, and other conventional concepts in the branches of human societal life.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological exposition of the functions of the human intellect (al-‘aql). It begins by asserting that the intellect is the faculty that discriminates between a wide range of opposites—moral, aesthetic, practical, and metaphysical. It then acknowledges that while the intellect draws much from the senses, it is productive, generating new knowledge not directly given by sensation. The analogy of a tree is used: just as a tree transforms raw elements into fruit, the intellect transforms sensory data into higher knowledge. The distinction between sense and intellect is clarified: the senses only transmit images of their objects, whereas the intellect moves from one perception to another through specific operations. Six such operations are enumerated: (1) Inference (istintāj): extracting a particular judgment from a universal judgment. This is considered the highest intellectual operation. For example, in Islamic jurisprudence, from the universal rule 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' one infers that a specific beverage containing alcohol is forbidden. (2) Perception of universal concepts (idrāk al-mafāhīm al-kulliyya): quoting Mulla Sadra, the author explains that knowledge passes through three stages: sensory, imaginal (khayālī), and conceptual. When one sees a beautiful landscape, the initial image is sensory; after leaving, the retained image is imaginary; when combining multiple such images, a universal concept (e.g., 'beauty') is formed. (3) Classification (taṣnīf): the mind groups existents according to shared attributes like stinginess, colors, etc. (4) Part-whole analysis (tajzi’a wa al-taḥlīl): breaking a single concept into its constituent concepts, e.g., analyzing 'animal' into 'rational' and 'non-rational,' or analyzing a physical volume into length, width, and height. (5) Synthesis (tarkīb): the mind combines two propositions to deduce a third, as in a syllogism. The example given is a causal statement: 'If fire is placed under a pot of water, the temperature rises.' In Islamic logic, this resembles a conditional syllogism. (6) Perception of creative concepts (idrāk al-mafāhīm al-ibdā‘iyya): these are conventional concepts like 'leader' and 'subordinate,' or 'lease' and 'lessor,' which are not found in external reality but are constructed by the mind for social organization. Throughout, the author emphasizes that the mind does not merely passively receive but actively processes and produces knowledge.

Key Terms
إدراك المفاهيم الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Sensation

D) Imaginal image

Key Takeaway
Universal concepts are not directly given by the senses; they are intellectual constructs formed by transforming and synthesising sensory images into abstract ideas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Describe how you would form the universal concept of 'patience' (ṣabr) using the three-stage process. Start with a specific instance of patience you witnessed.
?
Explain why Mulla Sadra calls this process a 'transformation' rather than just 'collection' of sensory images.

Synthesis (Tarkīb): Combining Premises to Produce New Knowledge
Definition and example of synthesis as combining propositions to deduce a third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a synthetic (syllogistic) argument in a conditional form and test its validity using modus ponens.

Core Idea
Synthesis is the operation where the mind combines two propositions to deduce a third, forming a decisive conclusion.

Synthesis (tarkīb) is the fifth operation listed. It is essentially the act of constructing a syllogism: taking two premises and deriving a conclusion. The author gives the example: 'If fire is placed under a pot of water, the temperature of the water rises.' This is a conditional statement, but the underlying logic can be seen as a form of hypothetical syllogism. In Islamic logic, synthesis is the core of qiyās (analogical reasoning), though here it is presented more broadly. This lesson will help you decompose such arguments and verify their validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions being combined
In the example, the first proposition is 'Fire is placed under a pot of water' (the antecedent). The second is 'The temperature of the water rises' (the consequent). These are the two parts of a conditional statement.
The author presents a single conditional sentence, but it implies two distinct propositions.
2
Determine the relationship between them
The relationship is causal: the first event causes the second. In logic, this is expressed as 'If P, then Q.' This is what the author means by 'combining two propositions'—they are not just any two, but ones linked by a conditional relation.
The combination yields a new proposition: the conditional itself, or the conclusion when one part is affirmed.
3
Deduce the conclusion using modus ponens
If we affirm the antecedent (fire is placed), we can validly conclude the consequent (temperature rises). That is the decisive result the author mentions. This is a valid form of inference.
Alternatively, denying the consequent would allow us to deny the antecedent, but that's not given here.
4
Relate to a classical Islamic argument
In Kalam, the contingency argument uses synthesis: 'If the world is contingent, then it requires a cause. The world is contingent. Therefore, it requires a cause.' Here, the two premises are combined to yield the conclusion.
Recognise this as a form of conditional syllogism (qiyās istithnā'ī muttaṣil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Linguistic Root of Ilham: Swallowing a Truth
Author explains the linguistic derivation of ilham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the etymology of 'ilham' to the root 'l-h-m' and explain how the physical meaning 'to swallow' metaphorically extends to the spiritual concept of inspiration.

Core Idea
Ilham literally means 'to swallow'—the soul swallows a truth cast into it.

The passage begins by establishing the literal meaning of ilham from the Arabic root 'l-h-m', which means 'to swallow'. This concrete image—swallowing a piece of food—serves as a powerful metaphor for how divine inspiration is received: as something thrown into the heart (al-ru') and then 'swallowed' or internalized. Understanding this etymological foundation helps you see why the term is so precise for describing immediate, unmediated knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root
Find the three-letter root from which 'ilham' is derived. The passage tells you it is 'l-h-m' (ل-ه-م), same pattern as 'fahm' (understanding).
2
Find the literal meaning
The root 'lahima' means 'to swallow' or 'to gulp down'. Notice the passage says: 'lahimtu al-shay wa iltahamtuhu' meaning 'I swallowed the thing and gulped it down'.
3
Connect to the spiritual meaning
The passage then says ilham is 'as if something thrown into the heart (ru') and swallowed'. The soul receives a truth from God and internalizes it immediately, like swallowing food.
This metaphor explains why ilham is knowledge without a process—it is ingested whole.
4
Observe the pre-Islamic rarity
The author notes that such terms were rare in pre-Islamic Arabic because psychological meanings were not common. This supports the idea that the term is precise for a spiritual concept introduced by revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدل على ابتلاع شيء ومن هذا الباب: الإلهام، كأنه شيء ألقي في الروع فالتهمه، والملهم هو: المفهم، الذي يصدق ظنه في الأشياء، تتكلم الملائكة على لسانه، فيجري عليه الصواب من غير قصد منه. وأصل معنى الإلهام من قولهم: لهم الشيء، والتهمه إذا ابتلعه، وألهمته ذلك الشيء أي أبلعته. قال الراغب الأصفهاني: الإلهام هو عملية استفاضة من اللوح المحفوظ تقوم بها النفس فتحصل العلوم فيها من خلال إشراق النفس الكلية عليها، إن أصول الأخلاق تتسم بسمة الثبات والخلود وهي من العلم الحضوري. قال تعالى: (وَنَفْسٍ وَمَا سَوَاهَا . فَأَلْهَمَهَا فُجُورَهَا وَتَقْوَاهَا)؛ قال عليه السلام: بين لها ما تأتي وما تترك. ويطلق الإلهام إطلاقاً خاصاً على حدوث علم في النفس من دون تعليم ولا تجربة ولا تفكير، فهو علم يحصل من غير دليل، سواء ما كان منه وجدانياً كالانسياق إلى المعلومات الضرورية والوجدانية، وما كان منه عن دليل كالتجريبيات والأمور الفكرية والنظرية، فهذا اللفظ اسم دقيق الدلالة على المعاني النفسية، وقليل رواج أمثال ذلك في اللغة قبل الإسلام لقلة خطور مثل تلك المعاني في مخاطبات عامة العرب، وهو مشتق من اللهم وهو البلع دفعة. ويقول بعض الفقهاء إن معرفة العلة هو إلهام. جاء في لسان العرب: أنها من مادة: لهم، على وزن فهم، أي ابتلاع أو شرب الشيء، والإلهام يعني التلقين الإلهي، ومع إلى أصل هذه الكلمة يمكن العثور على سبب الإطلاق، وكأن الروح تفتح فم الإنسان وتلقي فيه حقيقة فيمضغها. الإلهام أن يلقى الله في النفس أمراً يبعثه على الفعل أو الترك. العلامة النائيني قال: الإلهام هو الحد الوسط بين التكوين والتشريع. الإلهام علوم ذاتية حاصلة عن إخبار من الله بلا واسطة بين المخبر والمخبر. له الإلهام وحي باطن. الإلهام: النكث في القلوب. الإلهام إعلام سريع على وجه الخفاء. الإلهام: ما يلقى في الروع بطريق الفيض. الإلهام هو العلم اللدني الذي لا واسطة في حصوله بين النفس والباري. وقيل إن الإلهام خاطر حق من الحق تعالى.
It indicates swallowing something, and from this chapter: inspiration, as if something thrown into the heart and swallowed. The inspired one is the one who makes understand, whose opinion becomes true in matters, angels speak on his tongue, so correctness flows from him without intention. The original meaning of inspiration is from their saying: 'lahima' the thing, and 'iltahamahu' if he swallowed it, and 'alhamtuhu dhalika al-shay' means I made him swallow it. Al-Raghib al-Isfahani said: Inspiration is a process of overflowing from the Preserved Tablet undertaken by the soul, and sciences are acquired in it through the illumination of the universal soul upon it. The principles of morals are characterized by constancy and eternity, and they are from presentational knowledge. Allah said: 'And a soul and what proportioned it, and inspired it with its wickedness and its righteousness'; he (peace be upon him) said: He showed it what to do and what to leave. Inspiration is specifically applied to the occurrence of knowledge in the soul without teaching, experience, or thinking; it is knowledge obtained without proof, whether it is intuitive like the inclination to necessary and intuitive knowledge, or derived from proof like empirical, intellectual, and theoretical matters. This term is a precise indicator of psychological meanings, and such terms were rare in the language before Islam due to the rarity of such meanings in the discourses of the common Arabs. It is derived from 'lahm' which is swallowing at once. Some jurists say that knowledge of the cause is inspiration. It came in Lisan al-'Arab: that it is from the root 'l-h-m', on the pattern of 'fahm', i.e., swallowing or drinking a thing, and inspiration means divine dictation. And by looking at the origin of this word one can find the reason for its application, as if the soul opens the mouth of man and throws a truth into it and he chews it. Inspiration is that Allah casts into the soul a matter that impels it to action or abandonment. 'Allama al-Na'ini said: Inspiration is the intermediate between creation and legislation. Inspiration is self-acquired sciences resulting from a report from Allah without an intermediary between the reporter and the reported. For him, inspiration is inner revelation. Inspiration: the whisper in the hearts. Inspiration is a swift notification in a hidden manner. Inspiration is what is cast into the heart by way of emanation. Inspiration is innate knowledge which has no intermediary in its attainment between the soul and the Creator. And it is said that inspiration is a right thought from the Truth, Exalted is He.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive definition of 'ilham' (inspiration) from both linguistic and epistemological perspectives. The author begins with the literal meaning, tracing it to the root 'l-h-m' meaning 'to swallow,' suggesting that ilham is like swallowing a truth that is cast into the heart. The inspired person (mulhim) is one upon whom angels speak, and correctness flows without deliberate effort. Al-Raghib al-Isfahani is then quoted, defining ilham as an overflowing from the Preserved Tablet (al-Lawh al-Mahfuz) into the soul, mediated by the illumination of the universal soul. He notes that moral principles are part of presentational knowledge (al-'ilm al-huduri). The Quranic verse (91:7-8) is cited, and the commentary explains that Allah showed the soul what is good and evil. The author then narrows the definition: ilham is specifically knowledge that occurs without teaching, experience, or thinking—it is undemonstrated knowledge, whether intuitive (e.g., necessary truths) or derived from proof (e.g., empirical matters). The term was rare in pre-Islamic Arabic because such psychological concepts were not common. The passage then collects further definitions from various scholars: some jurists relate ilham to knowing the cause ('illa); Lisan al-'Arab gives the linguistic root; al-Na'ini sees ilham as intermediate between creation and legislation; others describe it as self-acquired knowledge from God without intermediary, inner revelation, a whisper in hearts, swift hidden notification, emanation into the heart, or innate knowledge (al-'ilm al-ladunni) with no intermediary between the soul and the Creator. The passage concludes with the notion that ilham is a true thought from God. Throughout, the author is assembling definitions to give a full picture of ilham as a source of direct knowledge.

Ilham as Direct Knowledge Without Reasoning: The Epistemological Definition
Author provides the specific epistemological definition of ilham.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ilham as knowledge that occurs without teaching, experience, or thinking, and distinguish it from other types of knowledge such as empirical and rational.

Core Idea
Ilham is undemonstrated knowledge, whether intuitive or derived, coming directly from God.

After the linguistic foundation, the passage provides a precise epistemological definition of ilham: it is knowledge that appears in the soul without any prior teaching, experience, or active thinking. This 'undemonstrated knowledge' can be either intuitive (like necessary truths) or derived (like empirical matters), but what matters is that it is not acquired through a deliberate reasoning process. This definition is crucial for understanding the role of inspiration in Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that states: 'يطلق الإلهام إطلاقاً خاصاً على حدوث علم في النفس من دون تعليم ولا تجربة ولا تفكير'. This is the core epistemological definition.
It uses the phrase 'without teaching, experience, or thinking'.
2
Identify the negative conditions
The definition excludes three sources of knowledge: teaching (ta'lim), experience (tajriba), and thinking (tafkīr). Ilham bypasses all of these.
Teaching implies a teacher; experience implies trial and error; thinking implies rational inference.
3
Classify as undemonstrated knowledge
The passage explicitly says: 'فهو علم يحصل من غير دليل' (it is knowledge obtained without proof). This distinguishes ilham from demonstrative knowledge ('ilm istidlali).
4
Note the two subcategories
The author divides ilham into two types: (1) intuitive (wijdānī) like inclination to necessary truths, and (2) derived from proof (like empirical and theoretical matters). This shows that ilham can cover both immediate and mediated insights, as long as the insight itself is not acquired through deliberate reasoning.
The term 'derived' here is tricky: it means the knowledge may correspond to what could be proved, but it is received directly, not deduced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدل على ابتلاع شيء ومن هذا الباب: الإلهام، كأنه شيء ألقي في الروع فالتهمه، والملهم هو: المفهم، الذي يصدق ظنه في الأشياء، تتكلم الملائكة على لسانه، فيجري عليه الصواب من غير قصد منه. وأصل معنى الإلهام من قولهم: لهم الشيء، والتهمه إذا ابتلعه، وألهمته ذلك الشيء أي أبلعته. قال الراغب الأصفهاني: الإلهام هو عملية استفاضة من اللوح المحفوظ تقوم بها النفس فتحصل العلوم فيها من خلال إشراق النفس الكلية عليها، إن أصول الأخلاق تتسم بسمة الثبات والخلود وهي من العلم الحضوري. قال تعالى: (وَنَفْسٍ وَمَا سَوَاهَا . فَأَلْهَمَهَا فُجُورَهَا وَتَقْوَاهَا)؛ قال عليه السلام: بين لها ما تأتي وما تترك. ويطلق الإلهام إطلاقاً خاصاً على حدوث علم في النفس من دون تعليم ولا تجربة ولا تفكير، فهو علم يحصل من غير دليل، سواء ما كان منه وجدانياً كالانسياق إلى المعلومات الضرورية والوجدانية، وما كان منه عن دليل كالتجريبيات والأمور الفكرية والنظرية، فهذا اللفظ اسم دقيق الدلالة على المعاني النفسية، وقليل رواج أمثال ذلك في اللغة قبل الإسلام لقلة خطور مثل تلك المعاني في مخاطبات عامة العرب، وهو مشتق من اللهم وهو البلع دفعة. ويقول بعض الفقهاء إن معرفة العلة هو إلهام. جاء في لسان العرب: أنها من مادة: لهم، على وزن فهم، أي ابتلاع أو شرب الشيء، والإلهام يعني التلقين الإلهي، ومع إلى أصل هذه الكلمة يمكن العثور على سبب الإطلاق، وكأن الروح تفتح فم الإنسان وتلقي فيه حقيقة فيمضغها. الإلهام أن يلقى الله في النفس أمراً يبعثه على الفعل أو الترك. العلامة النائيني قال: الإلهام هو الحد الوسط بين التكوين والتشريع. الإلهام علوم ذاتية حاصلة عن إخبار من الله بلا واسطة بين المخبر والمخبر. له الإلهام وحي باطن. الإلهام: النكث في القلوب. الإلهام إعلام سريع على وجه الخفاء. الإلهام: ما يلقى في الروع بطريق الفيض. الإلهام هو العلم اللدني الذي لا واسطة في حصوله بين النفس والباري. وقيل إن الإلهام خاطر حق من الحق تعالى.

Classifying and Critiquing Philosophical Definitions of Evil
Survey of definitions of evil from dictionaries and various philosophical schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify and critically evaluate different definitions of evil from various traditions, identifying their core claims and potential contradictions with Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Evil is defined not by consensus but by worldview — each definition reveals its source's ultimate commitments.

This passage compiles definitions of evil from dictionaries, mythologies, and Greek philosophers. At first glance, it seems like a mere list, but each definition carries a distinct metaphysical assumption. Your task in this lesson is not just to memorize these views, but to dissect them: What do they assume about God, matter, knowledge, and human purpose? By doing so, you will gain the tool to compare any definition against the Islamic criterion of wahy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source type
Determine whether each definition comes from a dictionary, a mythology, or a philosopher. Note the type of authority it claims (e.g., lexicographic, religious narrative, rational argument).
Dictionaries give conventional usage; mythologies embed theology; philosophers argue from reason.
2
Extract the core claim about evil
For each entry, state in one precise sentence what evil is. For example: 'Evil is the object of blame' (Lalande), 'Evil is a god to be appeased' (Loki), 'Evil is relative to good' (Heraclitus).
Keep the wording close to the original text.
3
Identify the underlying assumption
What does the definition imply about the nature of the world, God, or human beings? For instance, the mythological view assumes polytheism; Heraclitus assumes interdependence; Socrates assumes privation.
This step links each definition to a broader worldview.
4
Assess compatibility with tawhid
Consider whether each definition can be reconciled with the Islamic belief in one All-Good God who creates both good and evil as part of His wisdom. Which definitions are clearly incompatible?
Think about Quranic verses on divine justice and the purpose of evil.
5
Formulate an Islamic critique
Based on Islamic teachings (e.g., Qadr, divine justice), write a brief critique of one of the definitions. For example, critique the Socratic privation theory by arguing that evil is not mere absence but a created entity with a purpose.
Use concepts from classical Kalam texts.

Source Text

ومن أجل توضيح معالم الصورة نطرح هذا البحث المقتضب الخير والشر بين القرآن والفلسفة ورد في معجم لالاند ان الشر ( هو كل موضوع للاستهجان أو اللوم وفي معجم بولدون ( ان الشر هو ما يضاد (الخير وقد قسمت الآلهة على آلهة خير وآلهة شر فعند المصريين القدماء كان اله الشر هو ( سخمت ) وفي المجوسية الفارسية كان اله الشر هو ( آهور من ( وعند الجرمان القدماء كان اله الشر (لوكي ( وكانوا يعتقدون ان على الانسان ان يسترضيه حتى لا يسبب له الضرر وذلك بتقديم القرابين والاضاحي، بينما ترى البوذية ان الحياة شر في ذاتها. وفي الفلسفة اليونانية نجد هرقليطس يقول ان الخير والشر أمران نسبيان لا يوجد أحدهما الا بوجود الآخر. أما منيدس فيرى ان الوجود هو الخير واما الشر فأمر ظاهري صرف . وعند امباقليس ان الخير والشر يتصارعان فيما بينهما وان الصراع سينتهي الى انتصار الخير. أما السوفسطائية فان الخير هو النافع والشر هو الضار. فما يحقق اللذة هو الخير وما يجلب الالم هو الشر. أما سقراط فقد رأى ( ان الشر يصدر عن المادة وعما هو جسماني ولأن الله هو الخير لا يمكن ان يكون علة للشر. وان الشر هو جهل وعدم وسلب وعلى العكس منه نجد أبيقور يقول ان الخير هو السعادة العقلية الخالصة والسعادة
And in order to clarify the features of the picture, we present this brief research: Good and Evil between the Quran and Philosophy. It is mentioned in Lalande's dictionary that evil is 'every object of disapproval or blame.' In Baldon's dictionary, evil is 'what opposes good.' And the gods were divided into gods of good and gods of evil. Among the ancient Egyptians, the god of evil was Sekhmet. In Persian Zoroastrianism, the god of evil was Ahriman. Among the ancient Germans, the god of evil was Loki, and they believed that man must appease him so that he does not cause harm, by offering sacrifices. Whereas Buddhism sees that life is evil in itself. In Greek philosophy, we find Heraclitus saying that good and evil are relative, one does not exist except with the other. Menedes sees that existence is good, while evil is a mere appearance. For Empedocles, good and evil struggle with each other and the struggle will end with the victory of good. As for the Sophists, good is what is beneficial and evil is what is harmful. So what brings pleasure is good and what brings pain is evil. Socrates held that evil arises from matter and from the physical, and because God is good, He cannot be the cause of evil. And that evil is ignorance, non-existence, and privation. On the contrary, we find Epicurus saying that good is pure intellectual happiness and happiness...
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a survey of various definitions and philosophical perspectives on good and evil. The author begins by stating the purpose: to clarify the picture with a brief research contrasting Quranic and philosophical views. He first cites two dictionaries: Lalande defines evil as 'every object of disapproval or blame,' and Baldon defines it as 'what opposes good.' Then he moves to mythological and religious traditions: ancient Egyptians had Sekhmet as the god of evil, Persian Zoroastrianism had Ahriman, and Germanic tribes had Loki, requiring appeasement through sacrifice. Buddhism is noted for viewing life itself as evil. In Greek philosophy, Heraclitus posited good and evil as relative and interdependent. Menedes argued that only existence is good, evil being mere appearance. Empedocles saw a struggle between good and evil with eventual victory of good. The Sophists equated good with pleasure and benefit, evil with pain and harm. Socrates attributed evil to matter and the physical, asserting that God (the Good) cannot cause evil, and that evil is ignorance and privation. Finally, Epicurus defined good as pure intellectual happiness. The survey is left incomplete, but it illustrates the diversity of viewpoints, contrasting with the earlier Islamic definitions.

The Quranic Epistemology of Good and Evil
Author reviews definitions of good and evil from multiple philosophies and ends with Quranic perspective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Quranic epistemological approach to good and evil from philosophical approaches by analyzing the source of knowledge for each definition presented.

Core Idea
The Quran grounds good and evil in divine inspiration, not human reason.

Throughout history, philosophers and religions have defined good and evil in countless ways—pleasure, benefit, ignorance, class struggle. But the Quran offers a radically different foundation: good and evil are not human constructs but divinely placed knowledge. This lesson guides you through the various definitions to see how the epistemological source (human reason vs. divine revelation) shapes each view, culminating in the Quranic position.

Guided Path
1
Survey the definitions
Read through the list of definitions from ancient religions, Greek philosophers, modern thinkers, and Christianity. For each, note what is considered good and what is considered evil.
Group similar definitions (e.g., pleasure-based, reason-based, nature-based).
2
Identify the epistemological source
For each definition, ask: On what basis is this claim made? Is it reason, observation, tradition, or divine revelation? Mark whether the source is human or divine.
Most philosophical definitions rely on human reason; the Quranic one relies on divine inspiration.
3
Compare the foundations
Contrast the Quranic view ('knowledge placed in the intellect by Allah') with the philosophical views. How does grounding in divine inspiration change the nature of moral knowledge?
The Quranic view asserts certainty and objectivity; philosophical views are subjective and relative.
4
Formulate the Quranic position
Summarize the Quran's claim: good and evil are fixed opposites taught by God, not invented by humans. Use the verse 'And We showed him the two ways' (al-Balad 90:10) as evidence.
This positions moral knowledge as part of fitra (innate disposition).

الجزء الناقص في الانسان على سائر الاجزاء قال ارسطو (ان) صراع الخير والشر هو صراع بين جزء سام وجزء منحط في النفس) أما في العصر الحديث فيقول الفيلسوف أسبينوزا ( ان الشر شأنه شأن الخير ليس له حقيقة انه فقط تصور عقلي غير موجود في الواقع وان الشيء الواحد يمكن ان يكون خيرا او يكون شرا في الوقت نفسه ونحن نسمي الامر الذي نكرهه شرا وذلك بحسب ما يشعر به الشخص ديكارت قال ان الخير والشر هو صراع بين النفس والجسم الفيلسوف ليبنتس قال ان للشر فائدة في تحقيق الخير في العالم ( أما الفيلسوف كنت فقال ان الشر يصدر من طبيعة الانسان الحيوانية والخير يصدر من انسانية (الانسان فيما قال الفيلسوف شوبنهور (ان) العالم كله شر مطلق لان كل رغبة تنشأ عن نقص تؤدي الى الالم الى ان تشبع والرغبة لاتنتهي ابدا ( ثم جاء كارل ماركس فقال ان الشر موجود في الصراع بين (الطبقات ورأى فرويد ان الشر هو ما يعج به اللاشعور من عوامل تدمير (النفس بقي ان نذكر مفهوم الشر عند الفيلسوف هيدجر الذي قال ان الشر ملازم للوجود لان الانسان موجود للموت والوجود وجود فناء ) وجاءت المسيحية فقالت ) ان كل شر انما يصدر عن ارادة الانسان وهو الذي يختار الشر كما يختار الخير والشر ناتج عن اساءة استعمال الانسان لحريته في الاختيار. وان الشر او الخطيئة ليس جوهرا قائما بذاته بل هو اضطراب واختلال أما القرآن الكريم فقال ( ان الخير والشر هما معرفة ربانية الهامية ثابتة انزلها الله سبحانه في عقل الانسان ( وهديناه النجدينِ ( أي علمناه النقيضين مثال (الخير والشر ) (الفجور والتقوى)
And if Allah touches you with harm, there is no remover of it except Him, and if He touches you with good, then He is over all things competent. The poet says: If it is said which people are the evilest of tribes, Kulaib points with palms of fingers. And to clarify the features of the picture, we present this brief research: Good and Evil between the Quran and Philosophy. In Lalande's dictionary, evil is everything that is subject to disapproval or blame, and in Baldwin's dictionary, evil is what opposes good. The deities were divided into gods of good and gods of evil. Among the ancient Egyptians, the god of evil was Sekhmet; in Persian Zoroastrianism, the god of evil was Ahriman; among the ancient Germans, the god of evil was Loki, and they believed that man must appease him so that he does not cause harm by offering sacrifices and offerings. While Buddhism sees that life is inherently evil. In Greek philosophy, we find Heraclitus saying that good and evil are relative matters; neither exists without the other. Parmenides sees that existence is good, and evil is purely apparent. Empedocles says that good and evil struggle with each other, and the struggle will end with the victory of good. The Sophists say that good is the beneficial and evil is the harmful. What brings pleasure is good and what brings pain is evil. Socrates held that evil emanates from matter and what is corporeal, and because God is good, He cannot be the cause of evil. Evil is ignorance, absence, and privation. Contrary to him, we find Epicurus saying that good is pure intellectual happiness and pleasure, and evil is misery and sorrow. The Stoics took a unique stance: they say that evil exists only in particulars because the whole as a whole is good. Plato said that evil is the dominance of the deficient part of man over the other parts. Aristotle said that the struggle of good and evil is a struggle between a noble part and a base part in the soul. In the modern era, the philosopher Spinoza says that evil, like good, has no reality; it is only an intellectual conception not existing in reality, and the same thing can be good or evil at the same time, and we call what we dislike evil according to what the person feels. Descartes said that good and evil are a struggle between the soul and the body. Leibniz said that evil has a benefit in achieving good in the world. Kant said that evil emanates from the animal nature of man, and good emanates from the humanity of man. Schopenhauer said that the whole world is absolute evil because every desire arising from a lack leads to pain until it is satisfied, and desire never ends. Then Karl Marx came and said that evil exists in the struggle between classes. Freud saw that evil is what the unconscious teems with destructive factors of the psyche. Finally, we mention the concept of evil according to Heidegger, who said that evil is inherent in existence because man is a being-towards-death, and existence is a being-to-perdition. Christianity came and said that every evil emanates from the will of man, and he is the one who chooses evil as he chooses good, and evil results from man's misuse of his freedom of choice. Evil or sin is not a self-subsistent essence but rather a disorder and imbalance. As for the Holy Quran, it said: 'Good and evil are divine, inspired, fixed knowledge that Allah, exalted, has placed in the intellect of man' 'And we showed him the two ways' (i.e., we taught him the two opposites, examples: good and evil, wickedness and piety).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a survey of various definitions and concepts of good and evil (al-khayr wa al-sharr) across different cultures, philosophies, and religions, culminating with the Quranic perspective. The author begins by citing a Quranic verse and a line of poetry to introduce the theme. Then, starting from dictionary definitions, he proceeds chronologically and thematically. The author first notes that in ancient religions, good and evil were personified as deities (Egyptian Sekhmet, Persian Ahriman, Germanic Loki), requiring appeasement through sacrifices. Buddhism is presented as viewing life itself as evil. Moving to Greek philosophy, the author lists the views of several philosophers: Heraclitus (relativity of good and evil), Parmenides (only good is real, evil is apparent), Empedocles (struggle ending in good's victory), Sophists (good=beneficial, evil=harmful, equated with pleasure and pain), Socrates (evil from matter, ignorance, privation; God not cause of evil), Epicurus (good=mental happiness and pleasure, evil=misery), Stoics (evil only in particulars, whole is good), Plato (evil as dominance of deficient part), and Aristotle (struggle between noble and base parts of soul). The author then transitions to modern philosophers: Spinoza (good and evil have no reality, are relative and subjective), Descartes (struggle between soul and body), Leibniz (evil has a purpose for good), Kant (evil from animal nature, good from humanity), Schopenhauer (world is absolute evil due to unending desire), Marx (evil in class struggle), Freud (evil from unconscious destructive factors), and Heidegger (evil inherent in existence as being-towards-death). The Christian view is then presented: evil originates from human will and misuse of free will; sin is not a substance but disorder. Finally, the author presents the Quranic position: good and evil are divinely inspired knowledge placed in human intellect, as indicated by the verse 'And we showed him the two ways' (al-Balad 90:10), teaching the opposites of good and evil, piety and wickedness. The passage does not contain a logical argument but rather a comparative exposition; it is epistemological in that it surveys different knowledge claims about the nature of good and evil.

Key Terms
الخير
الشر
اللذة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Quranic view presented in the text, what is the source of knowledge of good and evil?
A) Human reason alone
B) Divine guidance placed in the intellect
C) Cultural traditions
D) Personal pleasure

Key Takeaway
The Quranic view grounds good and evil in divinely inspired knowledge, contrasting with philosophical definitions that rely on human reason, pleasure, or social constructs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of evil from Socrates (ignorance/privation) and the Quranic definition (divinely inspired knowledge). Write a short paragraph explaining the key epistemological difference: one relies on human reason and the other on divine revelation.
?
In the passage, the Stoics say 'evil exists only in particulars because the whole is good'. Identify the epistemological basis of this claim (human reasoning about the cosmos) and contrast it with the Quranic basis (direct divine teaching). Which one provides certainty, and why?

The Quranic Definition of Good and Evil
Author surveys philosophical views on good and evil then presents the Quranic perspective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic definition of good and evil as divinely inspired fixed knowledge and contrast it with philosophical definitions.

Core Idea
Good and evil are not subjective; they are fixed divine knowledge imprinted in the human soul.

This lesson examines the Quranic perspective on good and evil after surveying major philosophical views. Unlike philosophers who reduce good and evil to pleasure, social constructs, or psychological states, the Quran presents them as fundamental, divinely inspired knowledge embedded in human nature. Understanding this distinction is crucial for building an Islamic worldview.

Guided Path
1
Survey the philosophical definitions of evil
Identify the key philosophers mentioned (Stoics, Plato, Aristotle, Spinoza, etc.) and their claims about evil. What do they agree or disagree on?
2
Find the Quranic definition
Locate the author's statement: 'good and evil are a divine, inspirational, fixed knowledge that Allah instilled in the mind of man'. What verses support this?
Quran 91:7-10 and 49:7 are cited.
3
Compare and contrast
How does the Quranic view differ from the philosophical ones? What is the key difference regarding the source and permanence of moral knowledge?
Philosophical views often make evil relative or dependent on human perception; the Quran makes it objective and innate.
4
Recognize the implication for human responsibility
Imam Ali's saying clarifies that what one seeks forgiveness for is from oneself, and what one praises Allah for is from Him. This ties the knowledge of good and evil to moral accountability.

'But Allah has endeared to you faith and beautified it in your hearts, and has made hateful to you disbelief, wickedness, and disobedience' (Hujurat 49:7). And Imam Ali, peace be upon him, defines this criterion by his saying: 'Everything for which you seek forgiveness from Allah is from you, and everything for which you praise Allah is from Him, Exalted be He.' The conclusion: good and evil are a fixed divine knowledge in the core of human nature, insight into the lawful and forbidden. The term 'al-najdayn' in the Holy Qur'an cannot be limited to a single specific concept; rather, it can be said that it is a comprehensive term for all mental opposites, including: lawful and forbidden, good and evil, filthy and pure, error and correctness, and thus the term 'al-najdayn' can be generalized to thousands of opposites in the world of life and the world of thought and feeling, which can be called two ways. In Arabic language books: 'Al-najd: what is opposite to al-ghawr. Every elevated piece of land with a flat back is a najd, plural anjaad. An-najaad in this description is land that is elevated and solid.' He said: 'Camels when they ascend a hill, they shoot with the eye the distant najaad.' And it is said: here is the clear path, and the clear path is called najd. An affair that is clear is najd, and a clear path is hadi. Umayya said: 'And indeed the clear guide Muhammad has come to you, a guide on the paths of guidance, not sluggish.'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive survey of the concept of good and evil from various philosophical and religious perspectives, culminating in the Islamic/Quranic view. The author begins by presenting the views of ancient and modern philosophers: the Stoics, Plato, Aristotle, Spinoza, Descartes, Leibniz, Kant, Schopenhauer, Marx, Freud, and Heidegger, each offering a different definition of evil—whether relative, psychological, or metaphysical. Then the author turns to Christianity, which sees evil as a result of human free will and original sin. Finally, the author presents the Quranic perspective: good and evil are divine, inspirational knowledge implanted in the human heart. The Quranic verses (91:7-10 and 49:7) are cited, showing that Allah inspired the soul with both its vice (fujur) and piety (taqwa), and that He made faith beloved and disbelief hateful. Imam Ali's saying clarifies that what one seeks forgiveness for is from oneself, and what one praises Allah for is from Him. The passage then explains the term 'al-najdayn' (the two ways) from the Quran, arguing that it is a comprehensive term encompassing all mental opposites such as halal/haram, good/evil, pure/impure, right/wrong. The linguistic root 'najd' means high ground or a clear path, so 'al-najdayn' signifies the two clear ways of good and evil. The author emphasizes that good and evil are not subjective but divinely fixed in human nature, providing an innate insight into what is lawful and forbidden. This passage is definitional in nature, defining the nature of good and evil from the Islamic standpoint after critiquing other views.

التقوى

The Comprehensive Quranic Term 'Al-Najdayn'
Author explains the Quranic term 'al-najdayn' and its comprehensive meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of 'al-najdayn' as a comprehensive term for all mental opposites and identify its linguistic and conceptual basis.

Core Idea
'Al-najdayn' is not just good and evil but a term for all logical opposites in Islamic thought.

In the Quran, Allah says 'wa hadaynahu al-najdayn' (We guided him to the two ways). Traditional exegesis often interprets this as the paths of good and evil, but this lesson shows that the term 'al-najdayn' is far more comprehensive. It encompasses all mental opposites such as halal/haram, faith/disbelief, truth/falsehood, and countless others. The linguistic root 'najd' means a clear, elevated path, making 'al-najdayn' the two clear ways.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Quranic usage of 'al-najdayn'
Find the verse 'wa hadaynahu al-najdayn' in the passage. What does the author say it means initially? 'i.e., We taught him the two opposites'
2
Examine the linguistic origin
Read the linguistic explanation: 'najd' means high ground or a clear path. How does this meaning support the concept of two clear opposites?
The author quotes Arabic dictionaries and poetry.
3
Understand the author's expansion
The author argues that 'al-najdayn' cannot be limited to one concept but includes all mental opposites: halal/haram, good/evil, etc. Why does the author make this generalization?
Because the term is comprehensive and can be applied to thousands of opposites.
4
Apply the concept
Think of other pairs of opposites in Islamic thought (e.g., sunnah/bid'ah, haqq/batil). Can they be considered 'najdayn'? Why?
The author says it is a comprehensive term for all mental opposites.
