عقل الانسان ( وهديناه النجدينِ ( أي علمناه النقيضين مثال (الخير والشر ) (الفجور والتقوى) (ونفس وَمَا سَوْاهَا (٧) فَأَلْهَمَهَا فُجُورَهَا وَتَقْوَاهَا (۸) قَدْ أَفْلَحَ مَنْ زَكَّاهَا (۹) وَقَدْ خَابَ مَنْ دَسَّاهَا (ولكن الله حبب اليكم الايمان وزينه في قلوبكم وكره اليكم الكفر والفسوق والعصيان ( " ويحدد الامام علي -ع- هذا المعيار بقوله كل ما استغفرت الله منه فهو منك وكل ما حمدت الله عليه فهو منه تعالى) والخلاصة : ان الخير والشر معرفة ربانية ثابتة عند الانسان في صميم الخلقة بصيرة الحلال والحرام ان مصطلح النجدين الوارد في القرآن الكريم لا يمكن حصره بمفهوم واحد محدد بل يمكن القول انه مصطلح شامل لجميع المتناقضات العقلية ومنها : الحلال والحرام ، الخير والشر . الخبث والطيب . الخطأ والصواب ، وهكذا يمكن تعميم مصطلح النجدين الى آلاف المتناقضات في دنيا الحياة وعالم التفكير والشعور التي يمكن تسميتها نجدين . وفي كتب اللغة العربية (النجد: ما خالف الغور . وكل شرف من الأرض استوى ظهره فهو نجد، ويجمع على أنجاد، والنّجادُ في مثل هذه الصفة أرض فيها ارتفاع وصلابة، قال: قلائص إذا علون فدفدا ... رمين بالطرف النجاد الأبعدا ويقال: ها هنا الطريق الواضح، والطريق الواضح يُسمى نجداً، وأمر نجد واضح، وطريق نجد هاد، قال أمية: وقد جاكمُ النّجدُ النذير محمد ... دليل على طرق الهدى ليس يهمدُ
Pleasure and that evil is misery and sadness. The Stoics took a unique position: they say that evil only exists in particulars because the whole as a whole is good. Plato said that evil is the dominance of the deficient part of man over the other parts. Aristotle said that the struggle between good and evil is a struggle between a noble part and a base part in the soul. In the modern era, the philosopher Spinoza says that evil, like good, has no reality; it is only a mental concept that does not exist in reality, and the same thing can be good or evil at the same time, and we call what we hate evil according to what a person feels. Descartes said that good and evil are a struggle between the soul and the body. The philosopher Leibniz said that evil has a benefit in achieving good in the world. The philosopher Kant said that evil arises from man's animal nature and good arises from man's humanity. The philosopher Schopenhauer said that the whole world is absolute evil because every desire arising from a lack leads to pain until it is satisfied, and desire never ends. Then Karl Marx came and said that evil exists in the class struggle. Freud saw that evil is what the subconscious teems with of destructive factors of the soul. We still have to mention the concept of evil of the philosopher Heidegger, who said that evil is inherent in existence because man exists for death and existence is existence of annihilation. Christianity came and said that all evil arises from man's will, and he is the one who chooses evil as he chooses good, and evil results from man's misuse of his freedom of choice. And evil or sin is not an independent essence but a disturbance and disorder. As for the Holy Qur'an, it said that good and evil are a divine, inspirational, fixed knowledge that Allah, glory be to Him, instilled in the mind of man: 'And We guided him to the two ways' (i.e., We taught him the two opposites, e.g., good and evil, vice and piety) 'And the soul and Him who proportioned it, and inspired it with its wickedness and its righteousness; indeed he succeeds who purifies it, and indeed he fails who corrupts it' (Shams 91:7-10). 'But Allah has endeared to you faith and beautified it in your hearts, and has made hateful to you disbelief, wickedness, and disobedience' (Hujurat 49:7). And Imam Ali, peace be upon him, defines this criterion by his saying: 'Everything for which you seek forgiveness from Allah is from you, and everything for which you praise Allah is from Him, Exalted be He.' The conclusion: good and evil are a fixed divine knowledge in the core of human nature, insight into the lawful and forbidden. The term 'al-najdayn' in the Holy Qur'an cannot be limited to a single specific concept; rather, it can be said that it is a comprehensive term for all mental opposites, including: lawful and forbidden, good and evil, filthy and pure, error and correctness, and thus the term 'al-najdayn' can be generalized to thousands of opposites in the world of life and the world of thought and feeling, which can be called two ways. In Arabic language books: 'Al-najd: what is opposite to al-ghawr. Every elevated piece of land with a flat back is a najd, plural anjaad. An-najaad in this description is land that is elevated and solid.' He said: 'Camels when they ascend a hill, they shoot with the eye the distant najaad.' And it is said: here is the clear path, and the clear path is called najd. An affair that is clear is najd, and a clear path is hadi. Umayya said: 'And indeed the clear guide Muhammad has come to you, a guide on the paths of guidance, not sluggish.'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive survey of the concept of good and evil from various philosophical and religious perspectives, culminating in the Islamic/Quranic view. The author begins by presenting the views of ancient and modern philosophers: the Stoics, Plato, Aristotle, Spinoza, Descartes, Leibniz, Kant, Schopenhauer, Marx, Freud, and Heidegger, each offering a different definition of evil—whether relative, psychological, or metaphysical. Then the author turns to Christianity, which sees evil as a result of human free will and original sin. Finally, the author presents the Quranic perspective: good and evil are divine, inspirational knowledge implanted in the human heart. The Quranic verses (91:7-10 and 49:7) are cited, showing that Allah inspired the soul with both its vice (fujur) and piety (taqwa), and that He made faith beloved and disbelief hateful. Imam Ali's saying clarifies that what one seeks forgiveness for is from oneself, and what one praises Allah for is from Him. The passage then explains the term 'al-najdayn' (the two ways) from the Quran, arguing that it is a comprehensive term encompassing all mental opposites such as halal/haram, good/evil, pure/impure, right/wrong. The linguistic root 'najd' means high ground or a clear path, so 'al-najdayn' signifies the two clear ways of good and evil. The author emphasizes that good and evil are not subjective but divinely fixed in human nature, providing an innate insight into what is lawful and forbidden. This passage is definitional in nature, defining the nature of good and evil from the Islamic standpoint after critiquing other views.

Key Terms
النجدين
الخير
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the term 'al-najdayn' in the Quran is:
A) Limited to the concepts of good and evil
B) A comprehensive term for all mental opposites
C) Specifically refers to the paths of faith and disbelief
D) Refers to two specific geographic locations

Key Takeaway
'Al-najdayn' is a powerful Quranic term that encompasses all pairs of opposites, providing a framework for understanding duality in Islamic thought.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify three pairs of opposites from the following: 1) haqq and batil, 2) sunnah and bid'ah, 3) tawhid and shirk. For each, explain why they can be considered 'najdayn' according to the author's definition.

The Universality of Certainties in Islamic Sciences
Author asserts the universal scope of certainties across sciences and ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of certainties (yaqiniyyat) and explain why they are foundational for all knowledge.

Core Idea
Certainties are the bedrock of all Islamic and scientific knowledge.

The author argues that certainties cannot be limited to logic alone; they encompass mathematics, physics, biology, jurisprudence, ethics, and language. This lesson explores the scope and importance of these certainties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fields listed as certainties
Find all the disciplines mentioned in the passage that are said to be certainties. Notice the range from logic to language to fiqh.
2
Understand the function of certainties
The passage says certainties are a 'spur, foundation, and starting point' for knowledge. Reflect on what this means for building knowledge.
3
Distinguish certainties from relative values
Contrast the fixed nature of certainties with the changing nature of customs and traditions. Why does this distinction matter?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يمكن حصر اليقينيات بصورة نهائية بل يمكن القول بثقة ان جميع القوانين المنطقية بل حتى قوانين علم الرياضيات وقوانين علم الفيزياء وقواعد علم الاحياء وقواعد استنباط الاحكام الفقهية والقوانين الاخلاقية وقواعد اللغة تعد من اليقينيات . ان أهمية اليقينيات هو كونها تعد مهماز ومرتكز وقاعدة انطلاق لابد منها لاكتمال المعرفة البشرية. وهناك قيم نسبية تتغير بتغير الحضارات أو الزمن مثل العادات والتقاليد العرفية وهي من العلم المكتسب مثلا : اكرام المحسن فانه امر يستحسنه العقل ولا يتغير حكم العقل هذا ابدا وانما الذي يتغير بمرور الزمان هو وسائل الاكرام وكيفياته فالاصول الاخلاقية ثابتة والعادات والتقاليد التي ليست الا لباسا للاصول البصائرية هي المتغيرة ان هذه الاصول - البصائر - قد نزلت على جميع البشر من دون استثناء بطريقة الالهام بتسديد وتأييد من قبل الله سبحانه وتعالى كما يبدو واضحا في آيات القرآن الكريم . الخلاصة ؛ إن البصائر ليست هي المعقولات بل هي سبب معرفة المعقولات وفي الوقت نفسه هي المعيار والحجر الاساس لتشخيص الحق عن الباطل والصواب عن الخطأ ومجموعة عظيمة من القضايا البدهية يجدها في باطن عقله يذعن بها الانسان من دون حاجة الى إقامة برهان أو طلب دليل وقد أعطيت من الله الجميع البشر بالتساوي ويسلم بها كل انسان . فاذا طرحت امام الانسان قضية ما لايدري هل هي صحيحة وصادقة أو زائفة وموهومة يرجعها الى القضايا والمعارف البدهية – البصائر - فإن صدقت تلك كانت هذه قضية حقة وان لم تصدقها كانت قضية باطلة
It is not possible to limit certainties definitively, but it can be said with confidence that all logical laws, even the laws of mathematics, the laws of physics, the rules of biology, the rules of deriving juristic rulings, ethical laws, and language rules are among certainties. The importance of certainties is that they are a spur, a foundation, and a starting point necessary for the completion of human knowledge. There are relative values that change with changing civilizations or time, such as customs and conventional traditions, which are acquired knowledge. For example: honoring the benefactor is something the mind approves of, and this judgment of reason never changes; only the means and manner of honoring change over time. Thus ethical principles are fixed, while customs and traditions that are nothing but a garment for the insight-based principles are what change. These principles – the insights – have been sent down to all humans without exception by way of inspiration, supported and confirmed by God Almighty, as is clearly seen in the verses of the Noble Quran. Conclusion: The insights are not the intelligibles themselves, but rather they are the cause of knowing the intelligibles, and at the same time they are the criterion and the foundation for distinguishing truth from falsehood and right from wrong. They are a great collection of self-evident propositions that a person finds in the depth of his mind and assents to without needing to produce a proof or seek evidence. They have been given by God to all humans equally, and every human submits to them. So if a proposition is presented to a person and he does not know whether it is true and correct or false and illusory, he refers it to the self-evident propositions and knowledge – the insights. Then if it agrees with them, that proposition is true; and if it does not agree, it is false.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage expands on the concept of certainties (yaqiniyyat) and introduces the term 'basa'ir' (insights). The author first asserts that certainties cannot be exhaustively enumerated; they include all fundamental laws of logic, mathematics, physics, biology, jurisprudence, ethics, and language. These certainties are the necessary foundation for all knowledge. Then the author distinguishes between relative values (e.g., customs) that change over time and absolute ethical principles that remain fixed. The fixed principles are rooted in 'basa'ir' – innate, divinely inspired insights that are universal to all humans. The conclusion encapsulates that basa'ir are not abstract intelligibles but rather the criteria for judging truth and falsity. They are self-evident propositions that every person can access without proof. Any new proposition is evaluated against these innate criteria: if it matches, it is true; if not, it is false.

Key Terms
اليقينيات (al-yaqiniyyat): Certain, foundational truths that are beyond doubt and necessary for all knowledge.
القيم النسبية (al-qiyam al-nisbiyya): Values that change with culture and time, such as customs and traditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT listed among certainties?
A) Logic
B) Ethics
C) Customs
D) Language

Key Takeaway
Certainties are not limited to formal logic; they include all fundamental principles across sciences and ethics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List three categories of certainties mentioned in the passage that are used in Islamic jurisprudence. Then, choose one and explain how it serves as a foundation for knowledge.

Basa'ir: The Innate Criterion for Truth
Author introduces basa'ir as innate, divinely-endowed criteria for truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define basa'ir and explain how they function as the criterion for distinguishing truth from falsehood.

Core Idea
Basa'ir are divinely inspired self-evident propositions that serve as the ultimate test for any claim.

The author introduces basa'ir as innate, God-given insights that are the foundation for all truth. This lesson unpacks this concept and its role in evaluating propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of basa'ir
Find where the passage describes basa'ir as 'self-evident propositions given by God to all humans'. Note that they are not acquired but innate.
2
Contrast basa'ir with relative values
The passage says ethical principles are fixed, while customs change. Basa'ir underlie the fixed principles.
3
Understand the evaluative procedure
The passage explains: when a proposition is presented, refer it to basa'ir. If it agrees, it's true; if not, false.
4
Distinguish basa'ir from intelligibles
The author states that basa'ir are not the intelligibles themselves but the cause of knowing them. Reflect on this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is not possible to limit certainties definitively, but it can be said with confidence that all logical laws, even the laws of mathematics, the laws of physics, the rules of biology, the rules of deriving juristic rulings, ethical laws, and language rules are among certainties. The importance of certainties is that they are a spur, a foundation, and a starting point necessary for the completion of human knowledge. There are relative values that change with changing civilizations or time, such as customs and conventional traditions, which are acquired knowledge. For example: honoring the benefactor is something the mind approves of, and this judgment of reason never changes; only the means and manner of honoring change over time. Thus ethical principles are fixed, while customs and traditions that are nothing but a garment for the insight-based principles are what change. These principles – the insights – have been sent down to all humans without exception by way of inspiration, supported and confirmed by God Almighty, as is clearly seen in the verses of the Noble Quran. Conclusion: The insights are not the intelligibles themselves, but rather they are the cause of knowing the intelligibles, and at the same time they are the criterion and the foundation for distinguishing truth from falsehood and right from wrong. They are a great collection of self-evident propositions that a person finds in the depth of his mind and assents to without needing to produce a proof or seek evidence. They have been given by God to all humans equally, and every human submits to them. So if a proposition is presented to a person and he does not know whether it is true and correct or false and illusory, he refers it to the self-evident propositions and knowledge – the insights. Commentary
This passage expands on the concept of certainties (yaqiniyyat) and introduces the term 'basa'ir' (insights). The author first asserts that certainties cannot be exhaustively enumerated; they include all fundamental laws of logic, mathematics, physics, biology, jurisprudence, ethics, and language. These certainties are the necessary foundation for all knowledge. Key Terms
البصائر (al-basa'ir): Innate, self-evident propositions given by God to all humans, serving as the ultimate criterion for truth. [Note: In earlier Islamic philosophy, 'basa'ir' often means rational intuition; here it is explicitly endowed by divine inspiration.]
المعقولات (al-ma'qulat): Intelligible concepts derived by reason; the objects of rational thought.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of 'basa'ir' in evaluating new propositions?
A) They are the same as logical laws
B) They are the criterion against which propositions are measured
C) They are acquired through education
D) They change over time like customs

Key Takeaway
Basa'ir are the innate, divinely endowed criteria that allow humans to discern truth from falsehood without external proof.

The Linguistic Root of Fitra
Author explains linguistic meaning of f-t-r.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the root meaning of 'f-t-r' as 'splitting lengthwise' and connect it to the Qur'anic concept of creation.

Core Idea
The root f-t-r means splitting—creation is a 'splitting' into a predisposed form.

The passage begins with the Qur'anic verse about fitra and then delves into the linguistic origin of the term. Understanding this linguistic foundation is key to appreciating the depth of the concept of natural disposition in Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the root letters
Find the three-letter root of the word 'fitra'. The passage states it is 'f-t-r' (ف ط ر). Memorize these letters as they are the core of the meaning.
In Arabic, most words are derived from three-letter roots.
2
Understand the literal meaning
The passage says 'al-shiqq tulan' (splitting lengthwise). This is the primary meaning. Recognize that the root implies a splitting or cleaving open.
Example: 'fatar al-sayf' means the sword split.
3
Relate to the Qur'anic verse
The verse 'fatara al-nas' uses the verb form. The passage explains that it means creating something in a form predisposed for an action. So creation is akin to 'splitting' a thing into existence with a specific design.
Allah's creation is not random but purposeful.
4
Note the extension to creation
The author adds that 'fatar Allah al-khalq' means bringing something into existence and creating it in a form that is 'mutarashshihan' (predisposed) for an action. So fitra is the original disposition that inclines one toward certain actions.
This connects the linguistic root to the theological concept.

٦ - الفطرة قال تعالى (فأقم وجهك للدين حنيفا فطرة الله التي فطر الناس عليها لا تبديل لخلق الله) ماهي الفطرة ؟ ورد في كتب الشريعة الاسلامية شروحات عديدة وآفاق واسعة لشرح الفطرة وسنعرض أهمها ( الفطرة لغة : أصل الفطر ؛ الشق طولا . يقال فطر فلان كذا فطرا وأفطر هو فطورا وانفطر انفطارا . قال : هل ترى من فطور ؟ أي اختلال. وفطر الله الخلق وهو ايجاده الشيء وابداعه على هيئة ترشحت لفعل من الافعال فقوله : فطرت الله التي فطر الناس عليها . فاشارة منه تعالى الى ما فطر أي أبدع وركز في الانسان من طبائع) الفطرة اصطلاحا : وردت تعريفات عديدة أهمها الآتي: ١ - (هي الشعور الذاتي الذي يحسه الانسان من نفسه ، و لا يعرف له مصدرا و لا تفسيرا كالواخز الذي يشعر به حين يفعل أو يحاول أن يفعل أمر ما) ٢ - هي الطبيعة السليمة التي تكون عليها كل موجود اول خلقه قبل ان يصيبه عيب . ٣ - هي معلومات يمتلكها الإنسان مع قيام وجوده في هذه الدنيا وترتبط بالغرائز والميول، نظير أن يكون جائعاً فيأكل حتى يشبع وعطشاناً فيشرب الماء حتى يرتوي ٤- هي ، المبادئ والأفكار الموجودة في النفس قبل التجربة. ٥- هي القانون الإلهي الطبيعي الذي اهتدت به النفس الأولى، وتتابعت من بعدها الأنفس المشتقة منها والذي يحمل كالسمة اللازمة جيلا بعد جيل، وقرناً من بعد قرن، إلى أن تقوم الساعة. -٦ هي كيان النفسي يسيطر على وحدة تشمل الجسم والعقل والروح. أي المادة واللامادة ٧- هي شهوات الجسد ورغبات النفس وتأملات العقل وسبحات الروح. ونزوات الحس الغليظة وتأملات الفكر الطليقة ورفرفات الروح الطائرة ٦ - هي ناموس طبيعي ثابت يدفع المخلوق الى تتميم نواقصه ورفع حوائجه ويهتف له بما ينفعه ويضره في حياته ۷ - هي أحكام أكثر بداهة من كل أمر بديهي، لم يختلف فيها الناس ولن يختلفوا ولا يوجد مثلها في البداهة والوضوح، وهي من أوضح الضروريات وأبده البديهيات - وهي إيجاد الشئ في الخلق وإبداعه على هيئة مترشحة لفعل من الافعال ۹ - (هي) العلم بما ينبغي له فعله و ما لا ينبغي له فعله
6 - Al-Fitrah. Allah says: 'So set your face toward the religion, inclining to truth, the fitrah of Allah upon which He has created people. No change should there be in the creation of Allah.' What is al-fitrah? In the books of Islamic law, there are many explanations and broad horizons for explaining al-fitrah, and we will present the most important of them. (Al-fitrah linguistically: the root of f-t-r is splitting lengthwise. It is said: fatar fulan kadha faturan, and aftara huwa faturan, and infatara infitara. He said: 'Do you see any fatur?' meaning any disorder. And fatr Allah al-khalq is His bringing something into existence and creating it in a form predisposed for an action. So His saying: 'the fitrah of Allah upon which He created people' is an indication from Him, Exalted, to what He brought into existence and embedded in man of natural dispositions.) Al-fitrah idiomatically: numerous definitions have been given, the most important of which are the following: 1 - It is the subjective feeling that a person feels within himself, without knowing its source or explanation, like the prick of conscience he feels when he does or tries to do something. 2 - It is the sound nature upon which every existent is created at the beginning of its creation before it is afflicted with a defect. 3 - It is information that a person possesses from the moment of existence in this world, related to instincts and inclinations, such as when hungry eating until full, and thirsty drinking water until satisfied. 4 - It is the principles and ideas present in the soul before experience. 5 - It is the divine natural law by which the first soul was guided, and after it subsequent souls derived from it follow, carrying it as a necessary trait generation after generation, century after century, until the Hour is established. -6 It is the psychic entity that controls a unity encompassing body, mind, and soul, i.e., matter and non-matter. 7 - It is the desires of the body, the cravings of the soul, the reflections of the mind, the flights of the spirit, the coarse impulses of the senses, the liberated thoughts of the intellect, and the soaring wings of the soul. 6 - It is a fixed natural law that drives the created being to complete its deficiencies and fulfill its needs, and calls out to it what benefits and harms it in its life. 7 - It are judgments more self-evident than every evident matter, on which people have not differed and will not differ, and there is nothing like them in self-evidence and clarity, and they are among the clearest of necessary truths and the most self-evident of axioms. - And it is the bringing of a thing into being and creating it in a form predisposed for an action. 9 - It is (the) knowledge of what one ought to do and what one ought not to do.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive collection of definitions of the concept of fitrah (natural disposition) from Islamic sources. The author begins with a Quranic verse (Surah Ar-Rum, 30) to establish the primary reference. Then, the passage proceeds in two parts: first, a linguistic definition, and second, a series of idiomatic definitions. 

Linguistically, the root f-t-r is explained as splitting lengthwise, as in 'fatar' meaning to split. This is extended to creation: 'fatr Allah al-khalq' means Allah's act of creating something in a form predisposed for a certain action. The phrase 'fitrat Allah' in the verse is thus interpreted as the original disposition imparted by Allah. 

Then come the idiomatic definitions, numbered (though with some inconsistency in the original). The first definition describes fitrah as an innate subjective feeling, like a conscience. The second defines it as a sound nature before any defect. The third associates it with instinctual knowledge, like hunger and thirst. The fourth sees it as a priori principles. The fifth describes it as a divine natural law inherited by souls across generations. The sixth (first occurrence) defines it as a psychic entity unifying body and soul. The seventh discusses it in terms of desires, reflections, and aspirations. The second occurrence of 'six' (likely a misnumbering) defines it as a natural law driving fulfillment of needs. The second occurrence of 'seven' (again likely misnumbered) defines it as self-evident judgments. An unnumbered definition speaks of creation in a predisposed form. Finally, the last definition (numbered 9) defines it as knowledge of right and wrong. 

Each definition offers a different lens—psychological, theological, metaphysical, ethical—reflecting the richness of the concept in Islamic thought. The passage does not critique or compare these definitions; it simply enumerates them as important understandings.

D) Instinct

Key Takeaway
The linguistic root of fitra is 'splitting lengthwise', indicating a creation that is 'split open' or predisposed for a purpose.

The Many Idiomatic Definitions of Fitra
Author enumerates multiple idiomatic definitions of fitra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the various idiomatic definitions of fitra into psychological, theological, and ethical dimensions.

Core Idea
Fitra is described as innate feeling, sound nature, instinct, a priori principles, divine law, and moral knowledge.

After the linguistic definition, the author lists nine idiomatic definitions that reflect different scholarly perspectives. These definitions show the breadth of the concept and how it has been understood in various Islamic disciplines. Categorizing them helps clarify the different aspects of fitra.

Guided Path
1
List the definitions
Read through the numbered definitions (1-9) in the passage. Write them down in your own words, noting the key phrase that characterizes each. For example, definition 1: 'subjective feeling like conscience'; definition 2: 'sound nature before defect'.
The numbering may have errors (e.g., two definitions labelled 6 and 7), but treat them as distinct entries.
2
Identify the main theme of each
For each definition, identify whether it emphasizes psychology (inner feeling, desires), theology (divine law, creation), instinct (hunger, thirst), or ethics (knowledge of right and wrong).
Some definitions combine multiple themes.
3
Group them into categories
Create at least three categories: Psychological (definitions about conscience, desires, soul), Theological (definitions about divine law, creation, first soul), and Ethical (definitions about moral knowledge, self-evident judgments).
This grouping is not fixed; you may find other patterns.
4
Analyze overlapping themes
Notice that some definitions appear in multiple categories. For example, 'sound nature' can be both theological and psychological. Reflect on whether this multiplicity indicates a coherent concept or different uses by different scholars.
This step sharpens your analytical skills.

Parsing the Multiple Definitions of Fitra
Author enumerates various definitions of fitra and concludes with its characterization as an innate law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the multiple definitions of fitra presented in the passage and identify the logical roles each plays in characterizing the concept.

Core Idea
Fitra is a layered concept understood through diverse descriptions, not a single formula.

This passage presents a philosophical exploration of fitra through a series of definitions and descriptions. Rather than offering a single, compact definition, the author accumulates perspectives, showing that fitra encompasses innate knowledge, practical intellect, divine law, and more. This lesson will guide you through parsing these definitions and understanding how each contributes to a fuller picture.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first definition and its scope
Locate the first definition: 'It is the divine natural law by which the first soul was guided...' Note that it frames fitra as a universal, historical law governing souls across generations.
This is a metaphysical and cosmic characterization.
2
List subsequent characterizations and group them by theme
Find each subsequent phrase starting with 'It is' or similar. Group them into themes: innate ethical knowledge (e.g., 'knowledge of what he ought to do'), practical intellect, self-evident judgments, and the child's disposition to recognize God.
Some definitions overlap; look for distinct emphases.
3
Note the shift from definition to description
Observe that after listing many definitions, the author states fitra 'distances itself from definition and formulation and draws near to understanding and perception.' This indicates a meta-cognitive shift: the concept resists formal definition.
This is the author's concluding methodological point.
4
Distinguish between definitions with genus+differentia and mere descriptions
Some definitions, like 'knowledge of what he ought to do,' have a clear genus (knowledge) and differentia (of ethical obligation). Others, like 'lusts of the body, desires of the soul,' are descriptive enumerations without a clear logical structure.
This exercise sharpens your ability to identify proper definitions.
5
Synthesize the concluding characterization
The author concludes that fitra is 'the general law that applies to the world of things and does not change' or 'the special disposition with which man was created.' Notice that these are broad definitions that encompass many aspects.
This synthesis shows how multiple facets can be integrated.

نظرية المعرفة بين القرآن والفلسفة
الفطرة: هي القانون الإلهي الطبيعي الذي اهتدت به النفس الأولى، وتتابعت من بعدها الأنفس المشتقة منها والذي يحمل كالسمة اللازمة جيلا بعد جيل، وقرناً من بعد قرن، إلى أن تقوم الساعة.

- هي ناموس طبيعي ثابت يدفع المخلوق الى تتميم نواقصه ورفع حوائجه ويهتف له بما ينفعه ويضره في حياته

- وهي إيجاد الشئ في الخلق وإبداعه على هيئة مترشحة لفعل من الافعال
- هي العلم بما ينبغي له فعله و ما لا ينبغي له فعله
- هي ( العقل العملي ) كما هو ملاحظ في استعمال الجوارح والقوى في مصلحة بدن الانسان وعقل فيما يعود على النفس كمالاً
- ( إنها الشيء الذي لا يتبدل في الروح والجسد)
- (هي الخلقة والهيئة التي في نفس الطفل والتي هي معدة، ومهيأة لأن يميز بها مصنوعات الله ، ويستدل بها على ربه، ويعرف شرائعه ويؤمن به
- (هي) تلك الحالة الواعية الاصيلة في شخصية الإنسان التي من خلالها يهتدي إلى الاشياء وتنطوي على مجموعة من الميول والمعارف ركبت وركزت في أعماق الإنسان و تعد من لوازم وجوده
- هي من الألطاف التي خص الله تعالى بها الإنسان من بين جميع المخلوقات ، إذ إن الموجودات الأخرى غير الإنسان إما أنها لا تملك مثل هذه الفطرة المذكورة ، وإما أن لها حظاً ضئيلاً منها

Theory of knowledge between the Quran and philosophy. The innate disposition: It is the divine natural law by which the first soul was guided, and after it the derived souls followed one after another, and it is carried as a necessary characteristic generation after generation, century after century, until the Hour is established. - The psychic entity controls a unity comprising body, mind, and spirit, i.e., matter and non-matter. - It is the lusts of the body, the desires of the soul, the contemplations of the mind, and the flights of the spirit; and the coarse impulses of sense, the free reflections of thought, and the flutterings of the soaring spirit. - It is a fixed natural law that impels the creature to complete his deficiencies and raise his needs, and calls out to him what benefits and harms him in his life. - It are judgments more self-evident than every evident matter; people have not differed about them and will not differ, and there is nothing like them in self-evidence and clarity, and they are among the clearest necessities and the most self-evident of self-evidences. - And it is the bringing of a thing into creation and originating it in a form ready to perform an action. - It is knowledge of what he ought to do and what he ought not to do. - It is the practical intellect, as observed in the use of the limbs and faculties for the benefit of the human body, and it is intellect regarding what returns as perfection to the soul. - It is the thing that does not change in spirit and body. - It is the nature and disposition in the soul of the child, which is prepared and ready for him to distinguish thereby the created things of God, and to infer thereby his Lord, and to know His laws and believe in Him. - It is that original conscious state in the personality of man through which he is guided to things, and it comprises a set of inclinations and knowledges that are embedded and focused in the depths of man and are among the necessities of his existence. - It is among the kindnesses with which God Almighty has distinguished man among all creatures, for other existents besides man either do not possess such mentioned innate disposition, or have only a small share of it. - Imam Muhammad ibn Ali al-Baqir, peace be upon him, interpreted the innate disposition as knowledge, i.e., knowledge of all principles of creation. The innate disposition is a topic that distances itself from definition and formulation and draws near to understanding and perception. Consequently, we can say: the innate disposition is the general law that applies to the world of things and does not change. Or: it is the special disposition with which man was created, and...
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a collection of definitions and descriptions of the concept of 'fitra' (innate disposition) from a philosophical perspective, likely within an Islamic epistemological framework. The author begins by stating the overarching theme: the theory of knowledge between Quran and philosophy. The first definition presents fitra as the divine natural law that guides souls successively until the end of time. Then, a series of definitions follow, each highlighting a different aspect: fitra as a psychic entity encompassing body, mind, and spirit; as a combination of bodily desires and spiritual contemplations; as a fixed natural law driving the creature to fulfill needs; as self-evident judgments that all humans agree upon; as the creation of things in a ready form; as knowledge of right and wrong; as the practical intellect; as the unchanging element in spirit and body; as the innate disposition in a child to recognize God and His laws; as an original conscious state containing inclinations and knowledges; as a divine kindness distinguishing humans from other creatures; and finally, citing Imam al-Baqir, as the knowledge of all principles of creation. The passage concludes with a meta-reflection: fitra eschews rigid definition and is better approached through understanding and perception. It summarizes fitra as the universal, unchangeable law pervading all things, or the special disposition with which humans were created. The author thus treats fitra as a multifaceted concept that cannot be captured by a single formula, inviting the reader to grasp it intuitively.

Key Terms

العقل العملي

Understanding Fitra as Innate Knowledge
Author discusses fitra as innate knowledge, illustrating with Quranic and social examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of fitra (innate knowledge) from the passage and distinguish them from acquired knowledge based on the author's criteria.

Core Idea
Fitra provides universal, untaught knowledge that shapes human instincts, ethics, and social order.

The author presents fitra as an epistemological category: certain knowledge is present in humans from birth, not acquired through senses or reason alone. This lesson will examine the examples and reasoning used to support the existence of such innate knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about fitra's nature
Read the passage and find where the author states that fitra is knowledge outside the sphere of learning. Note the distinction he makes between innate and learned knowledge.
2
List the examples of fitra
Compile all instances the author gives: child avoiding fall, shame, social harmony, unity preference, physiological needs, attraction between sexes, etc.
3
Analyse the evidence used
Notice how the author uses Quranic stories (Solomon and the ant, Jacob's grief) and everyday observations to support the existence of fitra.
4
Differentiate from acquired knowledge
Based on the passage, explain why examples like reading, writing, or calculus are not fitra. Identify the criterion: fitra is found without effort or teaching.

١ سورة آل عمران الآية ۱٣
Theory of Knowledge between the Quran and Philosophy. It lies outside what is familiar in its description from the natural context. He said, the Most High: (And gathered for Solomon were his soldiers of the jinn and humans and birds, and they were set in ranks. Until, when they came upon the valley of the ants, an ant said, 'O ants, enter your dwellings so that Solomon and his soldiers do not crush you while they perceive not.' So he smiled, laughing at her speech.) 107 And this is an indication in another direction, deeply immersed in grief, when he saw the father of Joseph distancing from what is familiar in companionship between father and son (And he turned away from them and said, 'O my grief for Joseph!' and his eyes became white from sorrow, and he was suppressing [his anger].) And caution against danger is considered one of the original innate laws. For you see a child avoiding slipping from a height without anyone teaching him, and he screams the word 'shame' when his private part is exposed! Likewise, (Man by nature inclines to familiarity and harmony with others, so he is affected by the general customs of his society in the manner of dress or speech or..., and this effect is practically reflected in his behavior and actions in general.) 3 The poet Abu al-'Ala' al-Ma'arri said: 'If I were given immortality alone, I would not love solitude in immortality.' And in man there is a feeling that drives him to organize his family and social affairs. If you ask any person about his opinion on unity and division, he would not hesitate to answer that unity is good and division is evil, regardless of details and circumstances. And in the matter of functions and social rights among individuals, everyone believes in the principle of equality among them in rights and functions. Likewise, every inhabitant of the planet believes in the feeling of one human family. And among examples of fitra (innate nature) is man being characterized by what human nature necessitates: the need for eating, drinking, sleeping, urination, defecation, marriage, warding off cold, heat, and enemy, and enjoyment of marriage and good food and drink, and his perception of hunger and thirst, tenderness, softness, longing for homeland, and modesty. These are types of information that cannot be counted within the sphere of learning and teaching; rather, a type of information that man finds in his depths without effort. And you see the newborn born with love for what suits his body from foods and drinks; he craves the milk that suits him. Man and woman are another example: the difference in creation between them in physical composition for a profound wisdom, that each has a mission in life that befits him/her, his/her nature, and qualifications. Motherhood with all its characteristics, virtues, and hardships is the core of woman's innate mission. Notice that the attraction between man and woman does not change and will never change throughout time; it is something that runs in their veins and pours into each of the sexes their natural inclinations and instincts. For blood carries hormonal secretions from various endocrine glands, affecting the brain, nerves, and other organs. Indeed, part of every body is distinguished from its counterpart in the opposite sex. Therefore, feminine characteristics appear in a woman in her entire body composition, her form, ethics, thoughts, inclinations, and voice, just as masculine characteristics appear in a man in his body, appearance, voice, actions, and inclinations. These are natural rules that have not changed since the day God created man and will not change until the Hour is established. (Adorned for people is the love of desires from women and sons and heaped-up treasures of gold and silver and branded horses and cattle and tillage.) And from the system of fitra is the lack of inclination to direct contact between brother and sister. (1 Surah Al 'Imran, verse 13)
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion, classifying fitra (innate nature) as a source of knowledge. The author begins by stating that this knowledge lies outside what is normally described in a natural context, citing the Quranic story of Solomon and the ants as an example of instinctive behavior (the ant's caution). Then he references the grief of Jacob for Joseph as another example of natural emotion. He argues that caution against danger is an original innate law, observing that a child avoids falling without being taught and instinctively feels shame. Similarly, humans naturally incline to social harmony and are influenced by societal customs, as reflected in behavior. He quotes al-Ma'arri's poetry to illustrate the natural desire for companionship over solitude. The author then asserts that every person inherently values unity over division and believes in social equality and the concept of a global human family. He provides a list of examples of fitra: physiological needs (eating, drinking, etc.), protective instincts, emotional responses (longing, modesty), and the attraction between sexes. He emphasizes that these are not learned but found within. He discusses the biological and psychological differences between men and women as part of the innate order, citing Quranic verse on desires. The passage concludes that the system of fitra also includes prohibitions such as incest. Overall, the author uses empirical observations and Quranic references to support the idea that certain knowledge is innate and universal, not acquired through teaching.

Examples of Fitra as Innate Knowledge
Author enumerates examples of fitra (innate knowledge) that do not require learning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify examples of fitra (innate knowledge) and explain why they are not learned.

Core Idea
Fitra is the innate knowledge every human possesses without teaching.

The author provides a list of human needs and inclinations that are universal and do not require formal instruction. These serve as evidence that there is knowledge inherent in human nature. In this lesson, we will explore these examples and understand what makes them fitra rather than kasbi (acquired).

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples listed in the passage
Find the specific human needs and inclinations mentioned: eating, drinking, sleeping, urination, defecation, marriage, defense against cold/heat/enemies, enjoyment of marriage and pleasant foods, perception of hunger/thirst, tenderness, softness, longing for homeland, modesty.
All examples are from daily human life.
2
Categorize the examples
Group them into physical needs (eating, sleeping), emotional inclinations (longing for home, modesty), and defensive instincts (against cold, heat, enemies).
This shows fitra covers multiple dimensions of human nature.
3
Determine why these are not learned
These inclinations arise naturally without teaching; they are universal across cultures and appear spontaneously in all humans.
The author explicitly states they 'cannot be counted within the circle of learning and teaching'.
4
Distinguish fitra from acquired knowledge
Compare with knowledge that requires learning, such as mathematics, language, or technical skills. Fitra is innate and does not depend on instruction.
This distinction is crucial for epistemology in Islamic logic and theology.

This is relevant to epistemology in logic, as it concerns the origin of concepts and assents. All examples are from daily human life and are common across cultures.

The Innate Love of Perfection: An Inductive Argument
Author argues that love of absolute perfection is an innate law in all humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the inductive structure of the argument for the innate love of perfection and trace its implications for Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Man's endless pursuit of perfection points to a perfect Being who alone satisfies the soul.

In this passage, the author presents the love of perfection as an innate law, similar to the survival instinct. Rather than a formal syllogism, the argument proceeds by observing human behavior across history and individuals, drawing a general conclusion about human nature. This type of reasoning, known as induction (istiqra'), is commonly used in Islamic thought to establish fitra-based truths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
What is the author's main thesis about human nature? The love of perfection is innate in all humans.
The author states: 'All humans, without exception, have been created with love for perfection.'
2
Locate the evidence
The author lists examples: survival instinct, hatred of deficiency, people seeking wealth/knowledge/worship, and the observation that individuals are never satisfied with finite perfection.
Notice that the evidence covers different types of people and cases.
3
Recognize the inductive pattern
The author extrapolates from observed individual behaviors to a universal law of human nature. What makes this induction plausible? It is comprehensive, covering all individuals and sects, and shows that when a person finds a higher perfection, they abandon the lower.
Induction (istiqra') is a common method in Islamic sciences, but it typically yields probabilistic knowledge unless complete.
4
Trace the conclusion
The author argues that the true beloved must be absolute perfection without flaw. In Islamic context, this points to God. How does this follow? If humans are never satisfied by finite perfections, their goal must be the infinite Perfect.
The passage ends incomplete, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أمثلة القوانين الفطرية غريزة السعي للبقاء ورفض الموت والفناء كراهة النقص والعشق للكمال المطلق و الجمال المحض (فقد جبل جميع البشر من غير استثناء على العشق للكمال فمع التجول في الأدوار التي مرت بها البشرية واستنطاق كل فرد من أفرادهم وكل طائفة من طوائفهم نجد أن هذا العشق و الحب قد جبل في طينته ونجد أن قلبه متوجه نحو الكمال بل جميع حركاته ومساعيه ليست هي إلا لأجل هذا الحب الكامن في ضميره فهو يريد الوصول إلى الكمال ويطلب مشاهدة معشوقه ومحبوبه وكل يجد معشوقه في شيء فيطلب ذلك الشيء و يتفانى في سبيله فأهل الدنيا يحسبون الكمال في الثروة فيسعون للوصول إليها وأهل العلم في العلم وأهل الآخرة في العبادة وهكذا فالناس جميعهم يسعون نحو الكمال فإذا تصوروه في موجود أو موهوم تعلقوا به وعشقوه ولكن على الرغم من ذلك فليس عشقهم لهذا الذي ظنوه بأنه معشوقهم وهذا الذي توهموه وتخيلوه ليس هو كعبة آمالهم إذ لو رجع الإنسان إلى فطرته لوجد أن قلبه في الوقت الذي يُظهر العشق لشيء ما فإنه يتحول عن هذا المعشوق إلى غيره إذا وجد الثاني أكمل من الأول وهكذا بل إن نيران عشقه لتزداد اشتعالاً فالإنسان يحب الجمال فيصل إليه ولا يقتنع به بل يطلب أجمل منه وهكذا و يعشق القدرة فيصل إليها ثم لا يستقر بل يتزايد حبه إليها ولا تخمد نار اشتياقه أصلاً بل تزداد لهيباً وهذه الحالة تشمل جميع افراد البشرية من غير استثناء. ثم إن الإنسان إنما يعشق الكمال الذي لا عيب فيه ولا كمال بعده والعلم الذي لا جهل فيه والقدرة التي لاعجز فيها والحياة التي لاموت فيها أي
Among the examples of innate laws are the instinct for survival and rejection of death and annihilation, hatred of deficiency, and love of absolute perfection and pure beauty. For all humans, without exception, have been created with love for perfection. So when wandering through the stages that humanity has passed through and interrogating every individual of them and every sect of them, we find that this love and affection has been ingrained in their nature, and we find that their heart is directed towards perfection; rather, all their movements and endeavors are only for the sake of this love hidden in their conscience. He wants to reach perfection and seeks to see his beloved. Everyone finds his beloved in something, so he seeks that thing and devotes himself to it. The people of the world think perfection is in wealth, so they strive to attain it; the people of knowledge in knowledge; the people of the afterlife in worship; and so on. So all people strive towards perfection. When they conceive it in an existent or an imagined thing, they become attached to it and love it. But despite that, their love is not for that which they thought was their beloved, and that which they imagined and fancied is not the Kaaba of their hopes. For if man returns to his fitra, he would find that his heart, at the time it shows love for something, turns from this beloved to another if he finds the second more perfect than the first, and so on. Rather, the fire of his love increases. Man loves beauty, reaches it, but is not satisfied with it; rather, he seeks more beautiful. And so on. He loves power, reaches it, then does not settle; rather, his love for it increases, and the fire of his longing never dies down, but rather increases in flame. This condition includes all individuals of humanity without exception. Then man only loves perfection that has no flaw and no perfection after it, knowledge that has no ignorance, power that has no incapacity, life that has no death, i.e.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the love of perfection as an innate law. The author lists it alongside survival instinct and hatred of deficiency. He then argues that all humans are naturally inclined to love perfection. Through observation of history and individuals, this love is found in everyone. People seek different objects (wealth, knowledge, worship) but all are driven by the same underlying love of perfection. However, the author notes that if a person follows their fitra, they will never be satisfied with any finite perfection; they will always seek a higher one. This leads to the conclusion that the true beloved is absolute perfection without flaw — which in Islamic context is God. The argument is not a formal syllogism but a persuasive exposition using induction from human behavior.

Key Takeaway
The argument from the innate love of perfection is an inductive proof that human nature points toward an absolute, flawless being — in Islamic terms, Allah.

Heredity and Fitrah: The Prophetic Hadith on 'Irq
Citing hadith and equating 'irq with gene

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the hadith on 'irq (heredity) supports the concept of fitrah as an innate source of knowledge.

Core Idea
The hadith 'Choose carefully for your sperm' reveals heredity as part of fitrah.

In this lesson, we examine a Prophetic hadith about choosing spouses carefully due to hereditary traits. The author of the passage equates 'irq (inherited trait) with the modern concept of the gene, and uses this to argue that fitrah—our innate disposition—has a hereditary component. This challenges purely empiricist views of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Prophetic hadith
Locate the hadith in the Arabic: 'تخيروا لنطفكم فان العرق دساس'. Note its position in the passage: it is cited as a proof from the Sunna.
The hadith is about selecting a spouse for childbearing.
2
Understand the term 'irq'
The author explicitly defines 'irq as 'gene'. This equates a classical Arabic term with a modern genetic concept. Consider what this implies about heredity's role in human nature.
'Irq in classical usage means 'root' or 'origin', often used for heredity.
3
Connect to fitrah
The hadith is placed in a lesson on fitrah as a source of knowledge. How does heredity relate to fitrah? The author implies that fitrah includes innate inherited traits that guide behavior and development.
Fitrah is often understood as the original human disposition; heredity may be its physical manifestation.
4
Evaluate the argument
Does the hadith directly support fitrah as a source of knowledge? The author expands its meaning. Assess the validity: is it reasonable to use heredity as evidence for an innate knowledge source?
Consider the distinction between physical traits and knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوم إذا هدر العصير رأيتهم ... حمرا عيونهم من المصطار إن الفوارس يعلمون ظهوركم ... أولاد كل مقبح أكار ورد في السنة النبوية الشريفة قول النبي محمد ( ص ) ( تخيروا لنطفكم فان العرق دساس ) فان العرق تعني بالضبط الجين. وتقول الدراسات العلمية الحديثة ان للعامل الوراثي دور في تركيب شخصية الفرد وسلوكه العام و من الدراسات المشهورة في هذا المجال دراسة العالم جوست لونتاج عام ١٩٤٤م واستخدمت فيها سبعة مقاييس للجانب العصبي والفيسلويوجي تبين من خلالها العلاقة الواضحة بين الغضب وزيادة ضغط الدم وتبين ان تركيب الجهاز العصبي يتدخل كثيرا في استجابات الفرد للاحداث التي تحيط به وكذلك تبين وجود اثر للوراثة في الضعف العقلي وفي مستوى ذكاء الفرد ولابد من الاستدراك بالقول ان تركيب الجهاز العصبي لا يصنع المعرفة كما تقرر النظرية النسبية في المعرفة ولكنه يؤثر في نوعية السلوك واتجاهاته وحسب كذلك تلعب الغدد الصم ومدى كفاءتها دورا مهما فاذا حصل أي خلل في كمية افراز هذه المواد فان ذلك يؤدي الى تغيرات خطرة في مظهر الانسان وشكله وفي انفعالاته وذكائه ونمط شخصيته ومن اهم الغدد الدرقية والادرينالية والصنوبرية والتيموسية والنخامية والتناسلية . ان اتزان افرازات هذه الغدد الصم من شروط تكامل شخصية الفرد واتزان قراراته ان الغدد تؤلف منظومة غير منظورة من العلاقات وتتصل من قريب او بعيد بجميع اجهزة الفرد ومجالات انشطته الاجتماعية والنفسية الخلاصة : الفطرة لطف من الله سبحانه وهي كالبوصلة النفسية والجسدية الثابتة في صميم الخلقة مودعة في كل فرد من أفراد العالم تجعل الانسان يتحرك وينمو ويتطور داخل اطار ثابت وهي مصدر من مصادر المعرفة .
A people who, when the juice ferments, you see them ... red eyes from the strain. The horsemen know your backs ... children of every ugly person. It was reported in the noble prophetic Sunna the saying of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'Choose carefully for your sperm, for the inherited trait is penetrating.' For 'al-'irq' means exactly the gene. Modern scientific studies say that the hereditary factor plays a role in the composition of an individual's personality and general behavior. Among the famous studies in this field is the study of the scientist Gust Leun Tag in 1944, in which seven measures of the neural and physiological aspect were used, through which the clear relationship between anger and increased blood pressure was shown, and it was shown that the composition of the nervous system greatly intervenes in the individual's responses to the events surrounding him, and also the effect of heredity on mental weakness and the level of individual intelligence was shown. It is necessary to add that the composition of the nervous system does not create knowledge, as the relative theory of knowledge states, but it affects the type and directions of behavior only. Likewise, the endocrine glands and their efficiency play an important role. If any defect occurs in the amount of secretion of these substances, it leads to dangerous changes in the appearance and form of the human, and in his emotions, intelligence, and personality pattern. Among the most important glands are the thyroid, adrenal, pineal, thymus, pituitary, and reproductive. The balance of secretions of these endocrine glands is a condition for the integration of an individual's personality and the balance of his decisions. The glands form an invisible system of relationships and are connected, closely or distantly, to all the individual's organs and areas of his social and psychological activities. Conclusion: Fitrah is a grace from God, and it is like a fixed psychological and physical compass in the core of creation, deposited in every individual of the world, making man move, grow, and develop within a fixed framework. It is a source of knowledge.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage opens with a poetic reference, then cites a hadith urging careful selection of spouses due to hereditary traits ('irq = gene). The author then presents modern scientific studies demonstrating the role of heredity and the nervous system in shaping behavior and intelligence, but carefully notes that the nervous system does not create knowledge—it only influences behavior. The discussion extends to the endocrine glands and their impact on personality. The conclusion reframes these insights within an Islamic epistemological framework: fitrah (innate disposition) is a divine gift, a stable inner compass that guides human development and is a source of knowledge, distinct from sensory or rational knowledge. The lesson integrates empirical science with Islamic theology.

Key Terms
العرق
الجين
الفطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'al-‘irq' exactly mean?
A) A scientific study
B) A gene
C) A gland
D) A nerve

Key Takeaway
The hadith on 'irq is used to show that fitrah includes innate inherited components, challenging purely environmental theories of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find the hadith in the passage and write a one-paragraph analysis of how the author uses it to support the claim that fitrah is a source of knowledge.
?
Consider another hadith about fitrah (e.g., 'Every child is born upon fitrah') and compare how heredity fits in.

Physiology and Fitrah: Nervous System and Glands
Arguing physiology influences behavior but does not create knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the influence of physiology on behavior and the creation of knowledge, as argued by the author regarding fitrah.

Core Idea
Fitrah is a divine source of knowledge; physiology influences but does not create it.

The author discusses modern studies on the nervous system and endocrine glands, showing they affect behavior and personality. However, he strongly asserts that these do not create knowledge—contra the relative theory of knowledge. This leads to the conclusion that fitrah is a distinct source, a fixed compass from God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scientific claims
Find the reference to Gust Leun Tag's 1944 study: it showed a relationship between anger and blood pressure, and heredity's effect on intelligence. List what the passage says about the nervous system's role in responses.
The study used seven neural and physiological measures.
2
Spot the author's qualification
Locate the sentence: 'تركيب الجهاز العصبي لا يصنع المعرفة' (the nervous system does not create knowledge). This is a key philosophical stance. Contrast it with the 'relative theory of knowledge' mentioned.
The relative theory likely refers to epistemological relativism that grounds knowledge in physiological processes.
3
Recognize the role of glands
The author lists several endocrine glands (thyroid, adrenal, etc.) and says their balance is necessary for personality integration. How do they affect decisions? Note that they are part of the physiological system but do not create knowledge.
Glands are listed: الدرقية , الادرينالية , الصنوبرية , التيموسية , النخامية , التناسلية.
4
Extract the conclusion
Read the final paragraph: 'الفطرة لطف من الله... وهي كالبوصلة النفسية والجسدية الثابتة... وهي مصدر من مصادر المعرفة.' Summarize the definition of fitrah: a fixed compass and source of knowledge.
Fitrah is not created by physiology; it is a divine gift.

Source Text

A people who, when the juice ferments, you see them ... red eyes from the strain. The horsemen know your backs ... children of every ugly person. It was reported in the noble prophetic Sunna the saying of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him): 'Choose carefully for your sperm, for the inherited trait is penetrating.' Distinguishing Revelation (Wahy) as a Unique Source of Knowledge
Author defines wahy linguistically and terminologically, citing classical scholars, and lists its Quranic meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Islamic epistemological definition of wahy from ordinary perception and inspiration, and identify the philosophical shift in its definition.

Core Idea
Revelation is a special perception created by God, not derived from sense, intellect, or human instinct.

This lesson introduces the concept of wahy (revelation) as a distinct epistemological source in Islam. Unlike sense perception or rational deduction, wahy is a direct divine creation in the soul of chosen individuals. The author begins with linguistic roots, then presents terminological definitions from classical scholars, and notes the Quranic usages. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping the Islamic theory of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition of wahy
Find the meanings given: indication, inspiration, hidden speech. Note the connotation of swiftness from al-Raghib.
Linguistic analysis is the first step in defining any Islamic concept.
2
Extract the terminological definition
Wahy is a special perception connecting select humans to the unseen, not from sense, intellect, or fitra. It is a feeling created by God.
This distinguishes it from ordinary knowledge.
3
Distinguish wahy from other perceptions
It differs from the common intellectual feeling; it is a direct divine creation, not a natural cognitive process.
This underscores its epistemological uniqueness.
4
Compare the definitions of al-Mufid and al-Shirazi
Al-Mufid emphasizes covert notification; al-Shirazi introduces Neoplatonic emanation from the universal intellect.
Notice the shift from a kalam-based definition to a philosophical one.
5
Note the Quranic meanings
The passage mentions four meanings (though cut off). Consider how revelation in the Quran can mean inspiration, guidance, etc.
The Quranic usage broadens the semantic field.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوحي أو الايحاء ، لغة الاشارة والالهام والكلام الخفي وأوحى إليه : كلمه بكلام يخفيه عن غيره وأوحى إليه . وأوحى أوماً . قال الراغب : أصل الوحي : ألاشارة السريعة) الوحي اصطلاحا : وسيلة لارتباط ثلة ممتازة ومميزة من البشر بعالم الغيب ان الوحي أدراك خاص متميز عن سائر الادراكات وليس نتاج الحس ولا العقل ولا الفطرة انما هو شعور خاص أو أشارة بفعل ما يوجده الله تعالى في كائناته وهو يغاير الشعور الفكري المشترك بين أفراد البشر عامة وبتعريف الشيخ المفيد الوحي) هو الاعلام بخفاء بطريق من الطرق) وبتعريف صدر المتألهين الشيرازي (الوحي هو أفاضة العقل الكلي على النفس وهو أصرح وأقوى من الالهام استعمل الوحي في القرآن الكريم في معان أربعة
Wahy or ilham, linguistically: indication, inspiration, and hidden speech. 'Awḥā ilayh': he spoke to him with speech that he conceals from others. 'Awḥā ilayh' and 'Awḥā aw'aman'. Al-Raghib said: The origin of wahy is swift indication. Wahy terminologically: a means of connection for an excellent and distinguished group of humans with the unseen world. Indeed, wahy is a special perception, distinct from all other perceptions, and is not a product of sense, intellect, or fitra. Rather, it is a special feeling or indication of an action that God creates in His creatures, and it differs from the intellectual feeling common among humans in general. According to the definition of al-Shaykh al-Mufid: 'Wahy is notification in concealment by some means.' And according to the definition of Sadr al-Muta'allihin al-Shirazi: 'Wahy is the emanation of the universal intellect upon the soul, and it is clearer and stronger than inspiration.' Wahy is used in the Noble Quran in four meanings.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines wahy (revelation) as a distinct source of knowledge. The author begins with linguistic definitions: wahy means indication, inspiration, hidden speech, or swift sign. He then gives the terminological definition: a special means of connection between certain chosen humans and the unseen world, characterized as a perception that is not derived from sense, reason, or fitra—it is a direct creation of God in the soul. This distinguishes it from ordinary intellectual feelings. Two authoritative definitions are cited: al-Shaykh al-Mufid (d. 1022 CE) defines wahy as covert notification, and Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi (d. 1640 CE) defines it as the emanation of the Active Intellect on the soul, clearer and stronger than inspiration (ilham). The passage concludes by noting that the Quran uses wahy in four different senses, though the text cuts off before listing them. The lesson underscores the Islamic epistemological view that revelation is a unique, non-natural source of knowledge.

Key Terms
الوحي
الإلهام
العقل الكلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a source of wahy?
A) Sense perception (al-hiss)
B) Divine creation directly in the soul
C) Connection with the unseen world
D) Emanation of the universal intellect

Key Takeaway
Wahy is a unique, divinely created perception that connects select humans to the unseen, distinct from sense, intellect, and inspiration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The First Type of Divine Guidance: Showing the Way
Author defines the first type of Quranic guidance: showing the way.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first type of Quranic guidance (Hidāyat Irā'at al-Ṭarīq) and distinguish it from other types by its characteristic of showing the path without necessarily bringing to the destination.

Core Idea
Guidance as showing the path does not guarantee arrival; it makes the path clear.

In this passage, the author introduces the first of four types of divine guidance from the Quran. This type, called 'guidance as showing the way,' is a common form of guidance that Allah gives to all creation. Understanding this type helps us see that human responsibility remains even after guidance is given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the Arabic term 'هداية إراءة الطريق' in the passage. This is the first type of guidance discussed.
The term literally means 'guidance as showing the path'.
2
Extract the key characteristic
The passage explicitly states 'الهداية هنا إراءة الطريق دون الإيصال إلى المطلوب' – guidance here is showing the path without reaching the desired. This is the essential differentia.
3
Find the Quranic examples
The author cites two verses: 'I guide you to a straight path' and the story of Thamud who were guided but chose blindness. These illustrate that guidance was given but not accepted.
4
Understand the implication
This type of guidance does not force belief; it only clarifies the truth. The choice to follow remains with the individual, as seen with Thamud.
This distinguishes it from other types that may involve direct intervention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن طريق هذه الهداية يرتفع الانسان في مدارج الكمال ، وشواهد هذا النوع من الهداية في القرآن الكريم عديدة منها قوله تعالى: (أهدك صراطا سويا) وقوله تعالى: {وَأَما ثَمُودُ فَهَدَيْنَاهُمْ فَاسْتَحَبُّوا الْعَمَى عَلَى الْهُدَى فَأَخَذَتْهُمْ صَاعِقَةُ الْعَذَابِ الْهُونِ بِمَا كَانُوا يَكْسِبُونَ} ۲ الهداية هنا إراءة الطريق دون الإيصال إلى المطلوب. ب - هداية إنارة خاصة : لبعض البشر من باب الكرامة والتبجيل والتقريب وتتضمن مشاهدة معجزات مادية وروحية خارقة للعادة ومن أمثلتها مشاهدة نبي الله ابراهيم لعملية إحياء الطيور المذبوحة المقطعة وخروج ابراهيم عليه السلام من وسط النيران سالما معافى وقصة عزير الذي مر على قرية و هي خاوية على عروشها وشق البحر لنبي الله موسى وشفاء المرضى على يد نبي الله عيسى والاسراء والمعراج الى السماوات على البراق لنبي الله محمد - ص - وغيرها من الامثلة القرآنية وهذه الإراءة والإشهاد هي أقوى مراتب الهداية و البيان و أعلاها و أسناها
Through this guidance, man rises in the degrees of perfection. Evidences of this type of guidance in the Noble Quran are numerous, including His statement: 'I guide you to a straight path' and His statement: 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance, so the thunderbolt of humiliating punishment seized them because of what they used to earn.' Guidance here is showing the path without reaching the desired. B - Special illuminative guidance: for some humans as a form of honor, veneration, and drawing near, and it includes witnessing extraordinary material and spiritual miracles. Examples include Prophet Abraham witnessing the process of reviving the slaughtered and cut-up birds, Abraham emerging from the middle of the fire safe and healthy, the story of Ezra who passed by a village in ruin, the splitting of the sea for Prophet Moses, healing the sick at the hands of Prophet Jesus, the Night Journey and Ascension to the heavens on the Buraq for Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), and other Quranic examples. This showing and witnessing is the strongest rank of guidance and clarification, the highest and most sublime.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage defines the first two types of divine guidance according to the Quran. The author begins by stating that through this guidance man ascends in perfection. He then provides Quranic evidence for the first type of guidance, which is 'guidance as showing the path' (هداية إراءة الطريق). This type merely points out the right way without necessarily bringing the individual to the destination. The example given is the guidance Allah sent to Thamud: they were clearly shown the path but chose misguidance. The second type is 'special illuminative guidance' (هداية إنارة خاصة), which is reserved for some humans, particularly prophets, as an honor and a means of bringing them closer to Allah. This type involves extraordinary miracles—material and spiritual—such as the reviving of birds by Ibrahim, his safety in the fire, the story of Uzair (Ezra), the parting of the sea for Musa, the healing of the sick by Isa, and the Isra and Mi'raj of Prophet Muhammad. The author emphasizes that this witnessing of divine signs is the highest and most sublime form of guidance and clarification.

Key Terms
هداية إراءة الطريق (Hidāyat Irā'at al-Ṭarīq): Guidance as showing the path without necessarily bringing to the destination; the first type of guidance discussed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the characteristic of the first type of guidance?
A) It involves witnessing miracles
B) It is reserved for prophets
C) It shows the path without necessarily bringing to the destination
D) It is the highest form of guidance

Key Takeaway
The first type of divine guidance is simply showing the way; it does not force belief or guarantee arrival at truth.

The Second Type of Divine Guidance: Special Illuminative Guidance
Author defines the second type of Quranic guidance: special illuminative guidance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the second type of Quranic guidance (Hidāyat Ināra Khāṣṣa) as special illuminative guidance given to prophets and certain righteous individuals, characterized by witnessing extraordinary miracles.

Core Idea
Special illuminative guidance is the highest form, granted as honor through direct witnessing of divine power.

The author moves to the second type of guidance, which is far more elevated. This guidance, called 'special illumination,' is a gift from Allah to prophets and close servants. It involves witnessing miraculous events that affirm faith and honor the recipient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the Arabic phrase 'هداية إنارة خاصة' in the passage. This is the second type.
It translates to 'special illuminative guidance'.
2
Note the recipients
The passage says 'لبعض البشر من باب الكرامة والتبجيل والتقريب' – for some humans as a form of honor, veneration, and drawing near. This indicates it is not for everyone.
3
Identify the content of this guidance
It involves watching extraordinary material and spiritual miracles (مشاهدة معجزات مادية وروحية خارقة للعادة).
4
List the examples provided
The author lists several prophetic miracles: Ibrahim's reviving birds, his emergence from fire, Ezra's story, Moses parting the sea, Jesus healing the sick, and Muhammad's Isra and Mi'raj.
These are all extraordinary events beyond normal causation.
5
Understand its rank
The author concludes that this type is 'أقوى مراتب الهداية والبيان وأعلاها وأسناها' – the strongest and highest rank of guidance and clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن طريق هذه الهداية يرتفع الانسان في مدارج الكمال ، وشواهد هذا النوع من الهداية في القرآن الكريم عديدة منها قوله تعالى: (أهدك صراطا سويا) وقوله تعالى: {وَأَما ثَمُودُ فَهَدَيْنَاهُمْ فَاسْتَحَبُّوا الْعَمَى عَلَى الْهُدَى فَأَخَذَتْهُمْ صَاعِقَةُ الْعَذَابِ الْهُونِ بِمَا كَانُوا يَكْسِبُونَ} ۲ الهداية هنا إراءة الطريق دون الإيصال إلى المطلوب. ب - هداية إنارة خاصة : لبعض البشر من باب الكرامة والتبجيل والتقريب وتتضمن مشاهدة معجزات مادية وروحية خارقة للعادة ومن أمثلتها مشاهدة نبي الله ابراهيم لعملية إحياء الطيور المذبوحة المقطعة وخروج ابراهيم عليه السلام من وسط النيران سالما معافى وقصة عزير الذي مر على قرية و هي خاوية على عروشها وشق البحر لنبي الله موسى وشفاء المرضى على يد نبي الله عيسى والاسراء والمعراج الى السماوات على البراق لنبي الله محمد - ص - وغيرها من الامثلة القرآنية وهذه الإراءة والإشهاد هي أقوى مراتب الهداية و البيان و أعلاها و أسناها
Through this guidance, man rises in the degrees of perfection. Evidences of this type of guidance in the Noble Quran are numerous, including His statement: 'I guide you to a straight path' and His statement: 'And as for Thamud, We guided them, but they preferred blindness over guidance, so the thunderbolt of humiliating punishment seized them because of what they used to earn.' Guidance here is showing the path without reaching the desired. B - Special illuminative guidance: for some humans as a form of honor, veneration, and drawing near, and it includes witnessing extraordinary material and spiritual miracles. Examples include Prophet Abraham witnessing the process of reviving the slaughtered and cut-up birds, Abraham emerging from the middle of the fire safe and healthy, the story of Ezra who passed by a village in ruin, the splitting of the sea for Prophet Moses, healing the sick at the hands of Prophet Jesus, the Night Journey and Ascension to the heavens on the Buraq for Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), and other Quranic examples. This showing and witnessing is the strongest rank of guidance and clarification, the highest and most sublime.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage defines the first two types of divine guidance according to the Quran. The author begins by stating that through this guidance man ascends in perfection. He then provides Quranic evidence for the first type of guidance, which is 'guidance as showing the path' (هداية إراءة الطريق). This type merely points out the right way without necessarily bringing the individual to the destination. The example given is the guidance Allah sent to Thamud: they were clearly shown the path but chose misguidance. The second type is 'special illuminative guidance' (هداية إنارة خاصة), which is reserved for some humans, particularly prophets, as an honor and a means of bringing them closer to Allah. This type involves extraordinary miracles—material and spiritual—such as the reviving of birds by Ibrahim, his safety in the fire, the story of Uzair (Ezra), the parting of the sea for Musa, the healing of the sick by Isa, and the Isra and Mi'raj of Prophet Muhammad. The author emphasizes that this witnessing of divine signs is the highest and most sublime form of guidance and clarification.

Key Terms
هداية إنارة خاصة (Hidāyat Ināra Khāṣṣa): Special illuminative guidance given to some humans as honor, involving witnessing extraordinary miracles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is for all humans
B) It involves showing the path
C) It involves witnessing extraordinary miracles
D) It does not lead to destination

Key Takeaway
Special illuminative guidance is the highest form, granted to prophets and chosen servants, characterized by direct witnessing of miraculous events.
